[Page297+ 39]
20313 <head>SELECTION I.</head>
20314 <head>THE STORY OF NALA AND DAMAYANTĪ.</head>
20315 <p>§ 1. The trend of Aryan migration in India has been from the extreme north-
20316 west to the south-east, across the region drained by the Indus and its affluents, and
20317 called the Panjāb or Land of the Five Rivers; and again south-east, down the valleys
20318 of the Jumna and Ganges. One prominent group of Aryan immigrant tribes was
20319 that of the Kosalas, Videhas, and Magadhas, who settled in the {%lower%} valley of the
20320 Ganges, {%east%} of its confluence with the Jumna, in the districts now named Oudh
20321 and Behar, the Palestine of Buddhism.</p>
20322 <p>§ 2. Another group of tribes had their home on the upper Indus, in the north-
20323 west of the Panjāb. Theirs was the religion and civilization of which the Vedas
20324 are the monument. Later we find them advancing south-eastward, and establishing
20325 themselves on the {%upper%} course of the Jumna and Ganges, in Madhya-deśa, The
20326 Mid-Land. Foremost among them are the tribes of the Bhāratas, the Kurus, and
20327 the Panchālas. Here arose the system of Brahmanism; here the simple nature-
20328 religion of the Vedas developed into a religion of priests and sacrifices; here the
20329 Bhāratas attained a kind of religious primacy and the lustre of a great name,
20330 although gradually merging their tribal individuality with that of kindred tribes;
20331 here were fought the battles of the Bhāratas; and here, to ever-ready listeners, in
20332 school or forest-hermitage, at a sacrifice or a burial, were told the tales of these
20333 battles and their heroes. These are the tales that form the nucleus of the Great-
20334 Bhārata-Story ({@mahā-bhārata_ākhyāna,@} or, more briefly), the Mahā-bhārata.</p>
20335 <p>§ 3. These tales were probably first circulated in prose, until some more clever
20336 teller put them into simple and easily-remembered metrical form. The date of
20337 these first simple epics we do not know. They may well have existed several
20338 centuries before our era; but neither their language nor the notices of the Greeks
20339 afford any satisfactorily direct evidence upon the subject. Around this nucleus
20340 have been grouped additions,--historical, mythological, and didactic,--until the
20341 Great-Bhārata, as we now have it, contains over one hundred thousand distichs, or
20342 about eight times as much as the Iliad and Odyssey together.</p>
20343 <p>§ 4. Only about one-fifth of the whole poem is occupied with the principal
20344 story. This, in the briefest possible summary, is as follows. The two brothers,
20345 Dhritarāshtra and Pāndu ({@dhṛta-rāṣṭra, pāṇḍu@}), were brought up in their royal
20346 home of Hastinā-pura, about sixty miles north-east of modern Delhi. Dhritarāshtra,
20347 the elder, was blind, and so Pāndu became king, and had a glorious reign. He had
20348 five sons, chief of whom were Yudhishthira, Bhīma, and Arjuna. They are called
20349 Pāndavas, and are the types of honor and heroism. Dhritarāshtra's hundred sons,
20350 Duryodhana and the rest, are usually called the Kuru princes, and are represented
20351 as in every way bad. After Pāndu's death, his sons are brought up with their
[Page298+ 46]
20352 cousins. The kingdom devolved on Dhritarāshtra, who in turn made his nephew
20353 Yudhishthira the heir apparent.</p>
20354 <p>§ 5. Yudhishthira's exploits aroused the ill-will of his cousins, and, to escape
20355 their plots, the Pāndu princes went away to the king of Panchāla, whose daughter,
20356 Draupadī, became their common wife. In view of this strong alliance with the
20357 Panchālas, Dhritarāshtra thought it best to conciliate the Pāndus. So he divided the
20358 kingdom, and gave Hastinā-pura to his sons, and to his nephews a district to the
20359 south-west, where they built Indra-prastha, the modern Delhi. Here the Pāndavas
20360 and their people lived happily under king Yudhishthira.</p>
20361 <p>§ 6. On one occasion Dhritarāshtra held a great assembly of princes at his
20362 capital. The Pāndavas were invited and came. Yudhishthira was challenged to
20363 play with Duryodhana, and accepted. The dice were thrown for Duryodhana by his
20364 uncle Śakuni. Yudhishthira loses everything--wealth, kingdom, brothers, wife. A
20365 compromise, however, is made, by which the Pāndavas give up their part of the
20366 kingdom for twelve years, and agree to remain incognito for a thirteenth. With
20367 Draupadī, they retire to the Kāmyaka forest, on the Saraswatī.</p>
20368 <p>§ 7. For twelve years the Pāndu princes dwell in the wood. Many legends are
20369 told to divert and console them in their exile; and these stories, with the description
20370 of the forest-life of the princes, combine to make up the third or ‘Forest-book,’ the
20371 {@vana-parvan,@} which is one of the longest in the whole poem.</p>
20372 <p>§ 8. The thirteenth year arrived and passed. “Then in the fourteenth the
20373 Pāndavas demanded back their possessions, but received them not. From this arose
20374 the conflict. They overthrew the ruling house, slew prince Duryodhana, and then,
20375 although losing most of their warriors, they got back again their kingdom.” MBh.
20376 i. 61. 51 = 2280. Thus ended the Bhārata, doubtless, in its oldest and simplest form.</p>
20377 <p>§ 9. The poem, as we now have it, spins out the story of the combat through
20378 several books and through thousands of distichs. At length Yudhishthira is crowned
20379 in Hastinā-pura, and Bhīshma, the leader of the Kurus, although mortally wounded,
20380 instructs him, for about twenty thousand distichs, on the duties of kings and on
20381 other topics, and then dies. In the seventeenth book, the Pāndus renounce the king-
20382 dom, and in the next, the last, they ascend to heaven with Draupadī.</p>
20383 <p>§ 10. The Nala-episode illustrates very well how loose is the connection of the
20384 episodes in general with the main thread of the Bhārata. The story of Nala is one
20385 of those inserted in the third book (above, § 7), and its setting is as follows. Ar-
20386 juna had gone to the heaven of Indra to get from him divine weapons. The other
20387 Pāndavas, remaining in the forest with Draupadī, lament their brother's absence and
20388 the loss of their kingdom. Meantime the fierce and stout-armed Bhīma addresses
20389 his brother Yudhishthira, and offers to go out and slay their deceitful cousins.
20390 Yudhishthira counsels Bhīma to wait till after the thirteenth year, and is trying to
20391 calm his impetuous brother, when suddenly there arrives a mighty sage, Brihadaśwa.
20392 The holy man is received with honor and with the customary guest's-dish of milk
20393 and honey. When he is seated, Yudhishthira sits by him, bewails his sad lot, and
20394 asks, “Hast thou ever seen or heard of a man more luckless than I am ? I believe
20395 there never was one more unhappy.” “On this point,” replies the sage, “I will tell
20396 thee a story of a king who was even more luckless than thou. King Nala was once
20397 cheated out of his kingdom by a false dice-player, and dwelt in the forest, and
[Page299+ 49]
20398 neither slaves, chariot, brother, nor friend was left him; but thou art surrounded by
20399 heroes, brothers, and friends, and oughtest not to grieve.” Thereupon Yudhishthira
20400 begs him to relate the story at length. Accordingly, to console the king, and to
20401 show him that there is hope of regaining his kingdom, just as Nala regained his,
20402 Brihadaśwa begins as in the extract given in the Reader (1^3^).</p>
20403 <p>§ 11. CHAPTER 1. Nala was a prince of Nishadha. Damayantī was the
20404 lovely daughter of Bhīma, the king of Vidarbha. By the miraculous interposition
20405 of swans, the prince and princess become mutually enamored.</p>
20406 <p>§ 12. CHAP. 2. Bhīma accordingly holds a swayamvara (‘self-choice’) for
20407 her. The neighboring kings are invited, and she is permitted to choose for herself
20408 her husband from them. The chief gods hear of it and determine to go also. On
20409 their way, they meet Nala, who is bound on the same errand.</p>
20410 <p>§ 13. CHAP. 3. The gods request Nala to sue for them. Reluctantly consent-
20411 ing, he enters the chamber of the princess and tells how the gods desire her hand.</p>
20412 <p>§ 14. CHAP. 4. Damayantī refuses to listen to the arguments in favor of the
20413 gods. She desires that the swayamvara be held in the usual form, and that the
20414 gods be present, and informs Nala that she intends to show openly her preference
20415 for him. All this Nala reports to the gods.</p>
20416 <p>§ 15. CHAP. 5. The gods and kings assemble. The four chief gods assume
20417 the appearance of Nala. Unable to distinguish the real Nala, the princess, dis-
20418 tressed, prays to the gods, and they, in answer, resume their proper forms and
20419 peculiar attributes (see 14^12^ N.). Thereupon she chooses Nala. The kings express
20420 their sorrow, and the gods their delight. The gods give Nala the magic power of
20421 having fire and water whenever he wishes, and a wonderful skill in cookery. The
20422 wedding-feast is celebrated. Nala returns to Nishadha with his bride. They live
20423 happily, and have a son and daughter.--Here the extract in the Reader ends.</p>
20424 <p>§ 16. CHAPTERS 6-26. These tell of Nala's misfortunes, and their final happy
20425 issue. He loses everything, even his kingdom, by gambling, and wanders, hungry
20426 and half naked, in the forest. He is transformed into a dwarf, and becomes chari-
20427 oteer of Rituparna, king of Oudh. Damayantī, at her father's in Kundina, is led by
20428 certain tidings to suspect that Nala is at Oudh. By way of stratagem, she holds out
20429 hopes of her hand to Rituparna, if he will drive from Oudh to Kundina, some five
20430 hundred miles, in a single day, knowing well that only Nala's skill in horsemanship
20431 (see 1^4^) is equal to this task. Rituparna gets Nala to drive him thither through the
20432 air. Nala receives as reward perfect skill in dicing. His wife recognizes him by his
20433 magical command of fire and water, and by his cooking. He resumes his true form,
20434 plays again, and wins back all he had lost, and lives happy ever after.*</p>
20435 <p>§ 17. When Brihadaśwa had finished the story of Nala, Yudhishthira, pleased,
20436 asked him for perfect skill in dicing; and the hermit, granting him his prayer, departed.</p>
20437 <p>§ 18. This story is unquestionably one of the oldest and most beautiful
20438 episodes of the Mahā-bhārata. It was extremely popular in India; and to this is
20439 due the fact that it escaped the bad influence of Vishnuism, whose adherents have
20440 worked over the vast epic, changing and interpolating, and always for the worse.</p>
20441 <note n="299-*"><p>The student may read the entire story in any
20442 of the numerous translations--by Milman, Bopp,
20443 Rückert, and others. The most easily obtained is
20444 the very spirited English rendering of Edwin Arnold,
20445 in his {%Indian Idylls,%} Boston, Roberts Brothers, 1883,
20446 $1.00.</p></note>
[Page300-a+ 46]
20447 <p>§ 19. Even the Nala, especially in the first chapter, has suffered much from
20448 later hands; but on the whole it is one of the least corrupted episodes. Its antiq-
20449 uity is shown by the simplicity of manners implied in its incidents--the prince, for
20450 example, cooks his own food,--by the character of Indra (cf. 14^22^N.), and in other
20451 ways. See Holtzmann's {%Indische Sagen,%} Stuttgart, 1854, p. xiv. Bruce has
20452 attempted to reject definite passages throughout the poem, and has published, at
20453 St. Petersburg in 1862, a text of the Nala, reduced from 983 to 522 distichs.</p>
20454 <p>§ 20. The story begins at iii. 53. 1 (folio 58b) of the Bombay edition of 1877,
20455 and at iii. 2072 of the Calcutta edition of 1834.</p>
20456 <p>§ 21. THE METRE. The stanza ({@śloka@}) or distich consists of four octosyllabic
20457 verses ({@pāda@}). The first and second pādas form together a half-śloka or a line,
20458 divided at the middle by the caesura: likewise the third and fourth. The more
20459 important rules follow.</p>
20460 <p>{%a.%} Odd pādas end usually with a first epitrite, <gr></gr>, or antispast, <gr></gr>.</p>
20461 <p>{%b.%} Even pādas end in a diiambus, <gr></gr>, or second paeon, <gr></gr>.
20462 So the type of the half-śloka is <gr></gr>. But</p>
20463 <p>{%c.%} In no pāda may the syllables 2, 3, 4 form a tribrach, <gr></gr>, or anapaest, <gr></gr>;</p>
20464 <p>{%d.%} Nor, in the even pādas, an amphimacer, <gr></gr>.</p>

20594 <p>{@7^b^. niṣadheṣu,@} ‘among the Nishadhans’

20597 <p>--It is not known where Nishadha was.
20598 Vidarbha, the modern Berar, is at the head-
20599 waters of the Taptī, between Nagpore and
20600 the Nizam's Kingdom, and about five hun-
20601 dred miles from Oudh. The general run
20602 of the story makes it highly probable that
20603 Nishadha was between Berar and Oudh.
20604 From chapter ix., it would seem to have been
20605 north of Oujein ({@ujjayinī@}). From an allusion
20606 in the Śatapatha-brāhmana, ii. 3. 2, Weber
20607 thinks it is in the south (i. e. from Madhya-
20608 deśa). These premises give some ground
20609 for the inference that Nishadha was in the
20610 valley of the Sind, which traverses Gwalior
20611 State, Central India. On the Sind is Nar-
20612 wār; and local tradition connects this place
20613 with “King Nala” in a story whose leading
20614 features bear a striking resemblance to those
20615 of our poem. Finally, Nala is said, chap. xv.,
20616 to have reached Oudh on the tenth day after
20617 quitting Damayantī, and this time suits the
[Page302-a+ 49]
20618 distance--some two hundred miles--very
20619 well.</p>

20621 for {@kṣ,@} cf. Whitney 13 end. {@--satyavādī,@}
20622 nom. s., 440. --As a dependent cpd (1265),
20623 {@akṣapriya@} may mean, 1. ‘dice-beloved,
20624 lucky at gaming,’ or, 2. ‘dice-loving, friend
20625 of dice, fond of gaming’: on the whole, mg
20626 2 is perhaps to be preferred.</p>

20656 <p>{@13. akarot,@} 3d s. imf. of {@√1kṛ,@} 714.
20657 --‘He (in the matter of, i. e.) for the sake of
20658 children made the greatest effort,’ i. e. he
20659 performed pious rites, etc.; or else, ‘He had
20660 the matter of children greatly at heart, all
20661 intent (upon it).’</p>
20662 <p>{@14. tam,@} 495, 274a. {@--abhy-agacchad,@}

20664 {@gacchati@} in vocab.: this is 3d s. imf., 742.

20667 clension, 339: formation, see references in

20671 was also a demigod, as Nārada; and the
20672 {@brahmarṣi,@} or priestly sage.</p>

20995 cumstances, i. e. in all their goings and do-
20996 ings.’ Amend vocab. accordingly.</p>
20997 <p>--‘Saluting them, Maghavan then asked
20998 after the welfare unbroken and also disease-
20999 less of them two under all circumstances--
21000 he, the mighty one’: i. e. (cf. the principle
21001 explained 4^15^N.) ‘asked if it had gone con-
21002 stantly well with them and if they had been
21003 entirely free from sickness.’</p>
21004 <p>{@14-15. āvayos,@} 491. --Copulas omitted
21005 --2^12^N. --‘Of us two (there is) welfare,
21006 O god, under all circumstances, O lord: and
[Page306-a+ 48]
21007 in the whole ({@kṛtsne@}) world, O Maghavan,
21008 the princes (are) prosperous, O mighty one.’</p>
21009 <p>{@18-20@} are prefatory to the question
21010 proper in line 21^a^: ‘The princes (proleptic
21011 nom. independent)…, who (509) go to
21012 death by the sword (280)…, --this (501)
21013 imperishable world, i. e. heaven, (is--cf. 2^12^
21014 N. end) granting every wish of them, just as
21015 ({@yathā_eva@}) of me (it grants every wish).’
21016 The idea is, Since the brave warriors can
21017 here obtain their every wish, why do they
21018 not come up ?</p>

21455 <head>SELECTIONS II.-XXI.</head>
21456 <head>FROM THE HITOPADEŚA, THE ‘BOOK OF GOOD COUNSEL.’</head>
21457 <p>§ 22. The first book ever printed in Sanskrit was Kālidāsa's ‘Seasons’ ({@ṛtu-@}
21458 {@saṃhāra@}), edited by Sir Wm. Jones, and printed in Bengalī letters in 1792. The
21459 first Sanskrit book ever printed in Nāgarī letters was the Hitopadeśa. It was edited
21460 by Carey, and printed at Serampore in 1803. The publication was undertaken, said
21461 Henry T. Colebrooke, “to promote and facilitate the study of the ancient and
21462 learned language of India in the College of Fort William.” It was chosen as the
21463 first for this purpose because of its easy style and intrinsic interest and because two
21464 English translations of it existed, one by Wilkins (Bath, 1787), and the other by
21465 Jones (London, 1799). To pedagogical reasons, accordingly, may be assigned in
21466 great part, the importance of this work: it has become important in the West,
[Page311+ 49]
21467 because the text is so well suited for tyros in Sanskrit; and in the Orient, because
21468 of the intrinsic value of its contents.</p>
21469 <p>§ 23. The book belongs to the ethico-didactic class of literature, and is what
21470 the Hindus call a {@nīti-śāstra@} or ‘cónduct-work.’ The term {@nīti@} (see this in the
21471 vocabulary) came to have special reference to the conduct of kings in their domestic
21472 life and in their foreign relations; a {@nīti-śāstra@} is, accordingly, a ‘Prince's hand-
21473 book of political and social ethics,’ a kind of ‘Mirrour for Magistrates.’ The Hito-
21474 padeśa consists of mingled verse and prose. The verses are mostly proverbs and
21475 maxims--often of the choicest practical wisdom; and their validity is proved,
21476 illustrated, and enforced by the fables, which are in prose.</p>
21477 <p>§ 24. The frame in which the work is set is simple and meagre. The sons of
21478 King Sudarśana of Pāṭaliputra^1^ (Patnā) are ignorant and vicious. He therefore con-
21479 vokes the wise men and asks if any one is able to reform the princes. Viṣṇuśarman
21480 offers to do so, and accordingly takes them in charge, and relates to them the stories
21481 which make up the body of the collection.</p>
21482 <p>§ 25. The Hitopadeśa is not an original work, but, rather, an excellent com-
21483 pilation of ancient material. The time of its composition has not been even
21484 approximately determined. The palm-leaf MS. brought by Mr. Cecil Bendall from
21485 Nepāl was written in the year 493 of the Nepāl era or A. D. 1373. And Professor
21486 Peterson's^2^ old paper MS. from Jeypore is of about the same age. At present we
21487 can hardly say more than that the work is at least 500 years old. In the working
21488 over of the material, the metrical portions would naturally be changed less, on the
21489 whole, than the prose; and in fact, many of the proverbs can be traced back in their
21490 identical form to works of antiquity. And travellers report that just such proverbs
21491 are current to-day in the talk of the lower classes of India.</p>
21492 <p>The author or editor of this collection of fables, according to the colophon^3^ of
21493 the Jeypore MS., was named Nārāyaṇa, and his patron and publisher was the prince
21494 Dhavalacandra.</p>
21495 <p>§ 26. The sources of this compilation are expressly said (end of the preface,
21496 17^8^) to be “the Pañcatantra and another work.” The first part of this statement
21497 is borne out by the fact that, out of forty-three fables in the Hitopadeśa, twenty-five
21498 are found also in the Pañcatantra. The latter work, as its name implies, consists
21499 of five books; while the Hitopadeśa is divided into four, whose titles are given in
21500 the preface, 17^7^. The correspondence is as follows. The first book of the Pañca-
21501 tantra answers in its frame-work to the second of the Hitopadeśa and the second of
21502 the Pañcatantra to the first of the Hitopadeśa. Five stories from the third book
21503 of the Pañcatantra, along with seven from the first, are scattered through the last
21504 two books of the Hitopadeśa. From the fourth book of the Pañcatantra only one
21505 story, “The ass in the tiger-skin,” appears in the Hitopadeśa; and from the fifth
21506 book, only three. It thus appears that, in the main, only the first three books of
21507 the Pañcatantra were drawn upon by the author of the Hitopadeśa; and Somadeva,
21508 in his Kathā-sarit-sāgara (chapters 60-64, much of whose substance is from the
21509 Pañcatantra, i.-iii.), has followed a similar course.</p>
21510 <p>§ 27. What the “other work” is we can hardly say with entire certainty. In
21511 it ought to be found together--if the statement of the preface is accurate--at least
21512 <note n="311-1"><p>From this point the spelling of proper names
21513 will no longer be anglicized.</p></note>
21514 <note n="311-2"><p>See Preface to his Hitopadeśa, p. i, ii, v.</p></note>
21515 <note n="311-3"><p>Peterson's ed., p. 161: cf. p. iv, v.</p></note>
[Page312+ 57]
21516 the eighteen fables of the Hitopadeśa which do not occur in the Pañcatantra.
21517 Had the preface said “other works,” the problem would be easier. Thus the story
21518 of the two giants, iv. 9, may be traced to the Mahā-bhārata, and the prototypes of
21519 other single stories are doubtless to be found in one and another ancient collection.
21520 Professor Peterson^1^ thinks that the “other work” is the Nītisāra of Kāmandaka.
21521 The identification deserves further study.</p>
21522 <p>§ 28. The contents of the Pañcatantra have been made the subject of one of
21523 the most important contributions to the literary history of the world by the late
21524 Professor Benfey. His principal results were published in his {%Pantschatantra%} (1859,
21525 see above, p. xviii, no 8), and in his introduction to Bickell's edition of the {%Kalilag%}
21526 {%und Damnag%} (1876). The latter contains, pages VI-X, a brief résumé of these
21527 results. The summary given by Keith-Falconer (1885, see p. 315) is a systematic
21528 and lucid account of the history of the fables, and is the one most to be commended
21529 to English-speaking students. Some of the most important items follow.</p>
21530 <p>{@A.@} The Indian original. In the sixth century of our era, there existed in
21531 India a Buddhist Sanskrit work, in thirteen chapters, treating of the conduct of
21532 princes.^2^ Its doctrines were inculcated in the form of beast-fables, or stories in
21533 which animals play the part of human beings.</p>
21534 <p>{@B.@} This Indian original was translated by a Persian physician named Barzōī,
21535 into the Pehlevī, the literary language of Persia, by command of the Sassanian king,
21536 Khosru Anūshīrvān, called The Just (531-579 A. D.).</p>
21537 <p>§ 29. {@c1.@} Both the Indian original and its Pehlevī version are irrecoverably
21538 lost; but from the latter were made two very notable translations. The first was
21539 into Syriac, made about 570 A. D., and called {%Kalilag and Damnag%} after the two
21540 jackals, Karaṭaka and Damanaka, who figured prominently in the introduction of
21541 the Sanskrit original. A single notice of this version had been preserved in a
21542 catalogue of Syriac writings made by Ebed-jesus (died 1318), and published by
21543 Assemani at Rome in 1725. A Chaldean bishop, Georgius Ebed-jesus Khayyāth,
21544 on his way to the ecumenical council in 1870, stumbled upon a manuscript of this
21545 Syriac version in the episcopal library at Mardīn. Through the mediation of Ignazio
21546 Guidi in Rome, and by a wonderful combination of lucky accidents and persistent
21547 efforts, the existence of “the lost manuscript” was made known to the eager in-
21548 quirers in Europe,^3^ and at last published in text and German translation by Bickell.</p>
21549 <p>§ 30. {@c2.@} The second translation from the Pehlevī was the {%Kalilah and Dim-%}
21550 {%nah%} or {%Fables of Pilpay%} in Arabic, made by Abd-allah ibn al-Moqaffa, a Persian
21551 convert to Islam, who lived under the caliph al-Mansor and died about 760. This
21552 version was published, though not in the best recension, by Silvestre de Sacy at
21553 Paris in 1816, and an English translation of it was given by the Rev. Wyndham
21554 Knatchbull, Oxford, 1819.</p>
21555 <p>According to the Arabic introduction, Dabshelim ({@deva-śarman@}) was the first
21556 king of the Indian Restoration after the fall of the governor appointed by Alexander
21557 <note n="312-1"><p>See his Introduction, p. 29, 43, Notes, p. 3. The
21558 Nītisāra was edited by Rājendralāla Mitra, in the
21559 Bibliotheca Indica, vol. iv.</p></note>
21560 <note n="312-2"><p>Such was Benfey's conclusion. It was ques-
21561 tioned by Weber, {%Indische Streifen,%} iii. 437. Bar-
21562 zōī's Pehlevī version (B.) may have been based on
21563 several {%different%} works--among them a Pañcatantra.
21564 Indeed, from the second chapter of the Arabic Kalilah
21565 and Dimnah, ‘The mission of Barzōī’ (Knatchbull,
21566 pages 40-41; cf. Keith-Falconer, p. xxi), and from
21567 other evidence (Keith-Falconer, p. liv f), this is the
21568 much more probable view.</p></note>
21569 <note n="312-3"><p>The story of the discovery is told by Benfey,
21570 in Bickell's book, pages XII-XXIII, as also in vari-
21571 ous periodicals there cited, p. XXII note, e. g. {%Lon-%}
21572 {%don Academy%} for Aug. 1, 1871.</p></note>
[Page313+ 49]
21573 at the close of his campaign in the Panjāb, B. C. 326. When firmly established,
21574 Dabshelim gave himself over to every wickedness. To reclaim the king, a Brahman
21575 philosopher takes up his parable, as did Nathan before David, and at last wins him
21576 back to virtue. The wise man is called in Arabic {%bid-bah%}^1^ and in Syriac {%bid-vag.%}
21577 These words are satisfactorily traced by Benfey, through the Pehlevī, to the Sanskrit
21578 {@vidyā-pati,@} ‘master of sciences.’ Accordingly, {%bidbah,%} which has become Bidpai or
21579 Pilpay in our modern books, is not really a proper name, but an appellative, applied
21580 to a ‘Chief Pandit’ or ‘Court-scholar’ of an Indian prince.</p>
21581 <p>The Arabic version is of prime importance, since from it have flowed other
21582 versions, which have been of the utmost influence in shaping the literature of the
21583 Middle Ages.</p>
21584 <p>§ 31. {@D.@} These versions are: {@1.@} The Later Syriac, made in the tenth or
21585 eleventh century, edited by Wright, and translated by Keith-Falconer; {@2.@} the
21586 Greek one, made about 1080, by Symeon Seth, a Jewish physician; {@3.@} the Persian,
21587 made some fifty years later, by Nasr Allah of Ghaznī; {@4.@} the Hebrew, ascribed to
21588 Rabbi Joel, and probably made before 1250, and published, with French translation,
21589 at Paris in 1881, by Joseph Derenbourg; {@5.@} the old Spanish, made from the Arabic
21590 in 1251, and published at Madrid in 1860 by Gayangos.</p>
21591 <p>§ 32. {@E.@} Of the descendants in the fifth degree from the original, only two
21592 need be mentioned: {@3.@} The Persian {%Anwār-i Suhailī%} or ‘Lights of Canopus,’ a sim-
21593 plified recast of Nasr Allah's, made about 1494 by Husain Waiz al-Kāshifī. English
21594 translations of this have been published by Eastwick and by Wollaston, see below.
21595 {@4.@} The {%Directorium humanae vitae,%} made from the Hebrew about 1270 by John
21596 of Capua, and printed about 1480.</p>
21597 <p>§ 33. {@F.@} From John of Capua's version flowed the famous ‘Book of examples
21598 of the ancient sages,’ {%Das bu°ch der byspel der alten wysen.%} It was made at the
21599 instance of Duke Eberhard im Bart, whose name and motto, EBERHART GRAF Z[u]
21600 WIRTENBERG ATTEMPTO, appear as an acrostic in the initials of the first sections.
21601 It was first printed about 1481, and has since been admirably edited by W. L.
21602 Holland, Stuttgart, 1860. Holland used, besides three manuscripts, two printed
21603 editions {%sine loco et anno,%} and enumerates 17 dated editions that appeared between
21604 1483 and 1592. Four dated editions appeared at Ulm between 1483 and 1485 ! The
21605 great number of editions of the work and their rapid succession are the best proof
21606 of its importance as a means of instruction and amusement in the fifteenth and
21607 sixteenth centuries.</p>
21608 <p>Another offshoot from the {%Directorium%} is the Italian version of A. F. Doni,
21609 entitled {%La moral filosophia,%} and printed at Venice^2^ in 1552. This is of special
21610 interest, because from it came ({@G@}) the English translation of Sir Thomas North,
21611 London, 1570.</p>
21612 <p>It may here be added that La Fontaine, in the second edition of his Fables
21613 (1678), which contains eleven books, says^3^ that he owed the largest part of his new
21614 material (books vii-xi) to Pilpay, the Indian sage. The edition of Henri Regnier
21615 (Paris, Hachette, 1883-85, 3 vols.) gives abundant references to the sources of each
21616 fable, and is especially to be commended to those who would compare the well-
21617 known French offshoots with the Indian originals.</p>
21618 <note n="313-1"><p>See Benfey, in Bickell, p. XLIII f.</p></note>
21619 <note n="313-2"><p>With wood-cuts. Harvard College has a copy.</p></note>
21620 <note n="313-3"><p>{%Avertissement%} prefixed to book vii, Regnier ii.
21621 81.</p></note>
[Page314+ 52]
21622 <p>§ 34. From Benfey's investigations it appears that the truest extant repre-
21623 sentative of the Indian original is the Syriac version, {%Kalilag and Damnag.%} Next to
21624 the Syriac stands the {%Buch der Beispiele,%} which, besides, is in language remarkable
21625 for its dignity, strength, and beauty; upon this latter version, moreover, are based
21626 almost all the printed ones previous to 1644. To the German version almost exclu-
21627 sively, therefore, is Europe indebted for the wide-spread knowledge of this cycle of
21628 literature from the last part of the fifteenth to the middle of the seventeenth century.</p>
21629 <p>§ 35. After this account of the direct descendants of the Indian original in the
21630 Occident, it remains to speak of the history of that original in India, and of its
21631 sources. Whether Barzōī translated from one work of thirteen chapters (cf. § 28)
21632 or from several independent works, the fact remains that the originals of all of his
21633 sections may be certainly identified on Indian ground save three:^1^ five, namely,
21634 form the Pañcatantra; two other sections figure as a supplement to the first book
21635 of a later recension of the Pañcatantra; and yet other sections, three in number,
21636 appear in the Mahā-bhārata.</p>
21637 <p>The first three books of the Pañcatantra (above, § 26) were recast by Somadeva
21638 about 1070 A. D., in his Kathā-sarit-sāgara, chapters 60-64. Somadeva's abstract of
21639 these three books shows that they had the same form then as at the time of the
21640 Pehlevī translation (570). As representatives of the Indian original, the offshoots
21641 of the Pehlevī version surpass even the Indian offshoots. These latter, as respects
21642 their truthfulness in reflecting the Indian original, are arranged by Benfey as follows:
21643 first, the recension of the Indian original used by Somadeva; second, the one on
21644 which the Southern Pañcatantra (of Dubois) is based; third, the one from which
21645 the Hitopadeśa is made; and last, the one from which proceed the common Sanskrit
21646 recensions of the Pañcatantra.</p>
21647 <p>§ 36. At the time when Barzōī made his Pehlevī version, Buddhism was already
21648 on the decline in India, and Brahmanism regaining its lost supremacy. It was not
21649 to be expected on the one hand that the Brahmans would allow a work of such great
21650 artistic merit as the original {%Mirrour for Magistrates%} to be lost and forgotten, nor,
21651 on the other, that they would preserve it without transforming its whole spirit, which
21652 was that of fanatical hatred for Brahmanism. They have, therefore, omitted or
21653 transformed such parts as showed most Buddhist animus, leaving, however, many
21654 marks uneffaced which betray its Buddhist origin.</p>
21655 <p>In one other way, too, the original was modified. In most of its sections a
21656 doctrine was inculcated by means of a single fable or story, and only a sparing use
21657 was made of inserted apologues. But gradually the means became an end; into the
21658 main story were inserted others, and others still into these, until the main story
21659 became a mere frame, and the result was comparable to a set of Chinese boxes.^2^</p>
21660 <p>§ 37. Respecting the sources of the Indian original only a general statement
21661 can be made. There were current among the Buddhists, fables and parables which
21662 they ascribed to Buddha, and whose sanctity they sought to increase by identifying
21663 the best character in any story with Buddha himself in a former birth. Hence the
21664 tales were called {%Jātakas%} or ‘Birth-stories.’ There is evidence of the existence of a
21665 collection with that name as early as the Council of Vesālī, about 380 B. C.; and in
21666 <note n="314-1"><p>Of the remaining three, one is shown by its
21667 spirit of deadly hatred towards the Brahmans to be
21668 the work of Buddhists, and the other two are in
21669 Benfey's judgment genuinely Indian.</p></note>
21670 <note n="314-2"><p>Pedagogical reasons forbade the retention of
21671 this arrangement, except by way of specimen. Thus
21672 selection ix is boxed into viii, and xv and xvi into
21673 xiv.</p></note>
[Page315-a+ 54]
21674 the fifth century after Christ the Jātakas were put into the form in which they now
21675 appear in the Sutta-piṭaka. They are distinguished for quaint humor and gentle
21676 earnestness, and teach the duty of tender sympathy with animals and even of
21677 courtesy to them. With these stories may be identified many if not all of the
21678 fables of the Hitopadeśa.</p>
21679 <p>§ 38. The relation of the earliest Greek and Indian fables has been the subject
21680 of much discussion. Wagener tried to show that the Greeks derived their apologues
21681 from the Hindus; Weber, that the Hindus got many from the Greeks. Correspon-
21682 dences there undoubtedly are; but the difficulty is that the earliest forms of the
21683 fables--which would furnish the only safe basis for comparison--are irrecoverably
21684 lost. Aesop and his fables are mentioned by Plato and others as very well known:
21685 but whether he was a Phrygian, a Jew, or an Egyptian is matter of dispute; and
21686 even the <gr></gr> of Babrius (ca. 100 A. D.), which tradition offers us as the
21687 oldest extant collection, are removed some 700 years from the traditional date of
21688 Aesop. The collection on which the common modern fable-books are based was
21689 made by the Byzantine monk Maximus Planudes, ca. 1325.</p>
21690 <p>At all events, the oldest extant documentary collections of Greeks or of Bud-
21691 dhists are much later than Alexander's invasion; and considering the intercourse of
21692 the Greeks with India after that event, it is quite possible that the influence and
21693 borrowing were in both directions.</p>
21694 <p>§ 39. We have seen how, under the New Persian Dynasty, and afterwards
21695 under the Caliphs, with the spread of Islam, the Indian stories were carried over
21696 western Asia and all southern and western Europe. But this is not all. The pious
21697 pilgrims to India from China took home with them Buddhist apologues, which were
21698 translated into Chinese, and wandered then to Korea and Japan. They have since
21699 been translated from Chinese into French by Stanislas Julien ({%Les Avadânas,%} Paris,
21700 1859). Among the Mongols, too, Benfey has discovered many of these apologues;
21701 and through the Mongols during their supremacy these stories came to the Slavic
21702 peoples, and even to the Finns and Samoyeds.</p>

21752 <p>§ 41. THE LITERARY HISTORY of the Indian apologues has often been treated.
21753 So by Silvestre de Sacy, Loiselcur Deslongchamps, H. H. Wilson ({%Works,%} London,
21754 1864, iv. 1-159), Lancerean (in his Pantchatantra, Paris, 1871), Max Müller (On the
21755 migration of fables, {%Chips,%} London, 1875, iv. 145-209), Rhys Davids (Introduction
21756 to his translation of the Jātakas, London, 1880), J. Schoenberg (in the very readable
21757 introduction to his German translation of the Hitopadeśa, Vienna, 1884). Keith-
21758 Falconer's account is on the whole the best (§ 40).</p>
21759 <p>The French translation of the Hitopadeśa by É. Lancereau (Paris, Maisonneuve,
21760 1882) is especially useful on account of the full and convenient references to the
21761 books where the analogues of the different fables may be found. This book is
21762 intended by the citation “Lancereau.”</p>
21763 <p>§ 42. EDITIONS AND TRANSLATIONS. The most accessible text edition is that
21764 of Professor Peter Peterson (Bombay, Government Central Book Depot, 1887. Price
21765 1 rupee 10 annas). It contains an introduction most helpful for reading the text
21766 in course. It forms no. xxxiii of the Bombay Sanskrit Series. The text edition
21767 used most often as a standard of reference is that of Schlegel and Lassen (Bonn,
21768 1829).</p>
21769 <p>A literal English version has been given by Fr. Pincott (London, W. H. Allen,
21770 1880). A cheap reprint of Wilkins's translation appeared in Henry Morley's Uni-
21771 versal Library (London and New York, Routledge, 1885).</p>
21772 <p>§ 43. METRES. Aside from the common śloka--see p. 300, § 21--there occur
21773 in this book several other metres requiring description. The following five are
21774 monoschematic--i. e. the stanza consists of one metrical scheme four times repeated.
21775 The first two are simple and natural iambic-choriambic rhythms and are common in
21776 the Veda. The name {@vasanta-tilakā@} means ‘Grace of the spring-time’; {@mālinī,@}
21777 ‘Garlanded’; {@rathoddhatā@} seems to mean ‘Car-proud.’
21778 {%a.%} <gr></gr> {@jagatī,@} 22^11^.
21779 {%b.%} <gr></gr> {@triṣṭubh,@} 26^1^.
21780 {%c.%} <gr></gr> {@vasanta-tilakā,@} 18^14^, 26^8^.
21781 {%d.%} <gr></gr> {@mālinī,@} 22^22^.
21782 {%e.%} <gr></gr> {@rathoddhatā,@} 56^11^.
21783 The triṣṭubh is simply a catalectic form of the {@jagatī;@} but the catalexis gives the new
21784 cadence a trochaic effect instead of iambic. The rhythm of the {@vasanta-tilakā@} is
21785 closely analogous to that of the {@triṣṭubh.@} The {@rathoddhatā@} is essentially similar
21786 to a {@jagatī@} without its anacrusis.</p>
21787 <p>§ 44. THE {@āryā@} METRE is based on the number of morae--not on Thus:
21788 the number of syllables. See Whitney, §§ 76-79. A light syllable counts <gr></gr>
21789 as one mora; a heavy syllable, as two. In general, the {@āryā@}-foot con- <gr></gr>
21790 sists of four morae. These appear either as two heavy syllables, or with <gr></gr>
21791 one heavy one at the beginning or at the middle or at the end, or as <gr></gr>
21792 four light syllables. In each half-stanza, a caesura occurs at the end of <gr></gr>
21793 the third foot, and the eighth or last foot of each half-stanza is catalectic.</p>
21794 <p>The sixth foot of the first half-stanza must be an amphibrach, <gr></gr>, and that
[Page317-a+ 44]
21795 of the second must be a single light syllable. No odd foot may be an amphibrach.
21796 The metre occurs at 26^3^ and 56^5^ to 56^10^. The scheme is:
21797 <gr></gr>
21798 <gr></gr>
21799 Applying this scheme, for example, to 56^5-6^, we have, with the proper resolutions:
21800 <gr></gr>
21801 <gr></gr>.</p>

22142 <p>{@1.@} ‘Even yonder ({@asau@}) moon (sky-
22143 wandering, etc., p. 22) is eclipsed ({@grasyate@})
22144 ….’ The demon Rāhu got a part of the
22145 drink of immortality--cf. 32^12^N. The Sun
22146 and Moon betrayed him to Viṣṇu, who cut off
22147 his head. The head, grown immortal, avenges
22148 itself on Sun and Moon by swallowing them
22149 at times. Cf. introd. to selection xl.</p>
22150 <p>{@2.@} ‘Who is able to wipe out ({@√uñch@}) ?’
22151 The Hindus thought that every one's destiny
22152 was written on his brow, although invisible
22153 to human eye. Like enough the sutures
22154 of the skull, which look very similar to the
22155 written characters of some Indian alphabets,
22156 gave rise to this belief. Now-a-days, writing
22157 materials are placed beside the new-born
22158 babe in the lying-in room, in order that
22159 Vidhātṛ may write the child's destiny on its
22160 forehead. See Shib Chunder Bose, The
22161 Hindoos as they are, 2d ed., p. 25.</p>

22484 <p>{@19.@} SELECTION VIII. The crows and
22485 the serpent. Hitopadeśa, book ii., fable 10.
22486 --It is told by Damanaka to Karaṭaka (see
22487 p. 312, § 29), and into it is inserted the story
22488 of the lion, the old hare, and the well (selec-
22489 tion ix., see 32^12^N.), told by the crow to the
22490 crow-hen.</p>
22491 <p>--Selection viii. answers to Pañcatantra,
22492 book i., fable 6, which is told in like manner
22493 by Damanaka. He makes the crow and
22494 crow-hen ask a jackal for advice, and the
22495 latter tells them the inserted story of the
22496 heron, the fishes, and the crab (= selection
22497 xviii.). Damanaka then finishes his story
22498 about the crows (= selection viii.), and fur-
22499 ther enforces its moral by the story of the
22500 lion (selection ix.). The {%motif%} of selection
22501 viii. is analogous to that of selection xvi.,
22502 cf. 39^8^N.--A few references for further
22503 comparison:</p>

22530 <p>{@12.@} SELECTION IX. The lion, the old hare,
22531 and the well. Hitopadeśa, book ii., fable
22532 11.--See 31^19^N.</p>
22533 <p>--Selection ix. answers to Pañcatantra,
22534 book i., fable 8. The Buddhist version, the
22535 Banyan Deer Jātaka, has deep moral sig-
22536 nificance as showing the beauty of self-
22537 sacrifice and the excellence of loving “all
22538 things, both great and small.” This Jātaka
22539 is mentioned by name and illustrated on the
22540 bas-reliefs of the tope or {%Stūpa of Bharhut,%}
22541 Cunningham, plate xxv, dating from 200
22542 B. C. or earlier. In the Sanskrit forms, the
22543 Jātaka has developed into a simple story
22544 that shows how the weak animals get the
22545 better of a powerful tyrant, not by force,
22546 but by cunning. Somadeva gives the story
22547 at lx. 91-107. Compare further</p>
22548 <p>Pāli. Jātaka, no. 12: translation, i. p. 205.</p>
22549 <p>Old Syriac version. Kalilag, p. 14.</p>
22550 <p>Arabic. Kalila. Knatchbull, p. 117. Wolff, i. 46.</p>
22551 <p>Later Syriac version, p. 27.</p>
22552 <p>Anvār-i Suhailī, book i., story 14.</p>
22553 <p>Directorium, Cap. II., p. 10 of signature c.</p>
22554 <p>Buch der Beispiele, p. 37^1^.</p>
22555 <p>Benfey, i. 179; ii. 62. Lancereau, p. 333.</p>
22556 <p>--The first three phrases of this line (12)
22557 are specimens of the regular conventional
22558 way of introducing the fables. --The myth-
[Page325-a+ 48]
22559 ical mount Mandara was used by the gods
22560 as a churning-stick when they churned the
22561 ocean to get the drink of immortality--cf.
22562 23^1^N. In this book, a real hill may be in-
22563 tended, namely Mandara, a little south of
22564 Bhāgalpur, on the Ganges, cast of Magadha.</p>
22565 <p>{@14.@} See {@√dhā + vi5@} and {@√ās3.@} --Note that
22566 the gerund {@kṛtvā@} goes with the logical sub-
22567 ject of the sentence.</p>

22646 <p>{@12.@} SELECTION XI. The ass in the tiger-
22647 skin. Hitopadeśa, book iii., fable 3.</p>
22648 <p>--This story corresponds to Pañcatantra,
22649 book iv., fable 7, which has been worked
22650 over by Somadeva, lxii. 19-23. Its oldest
22651 Hindu form is the Lion-skin Jātaka. It
22652 seems to have been lacking in the Pehlevī
22653 version. Plato makes Socrates say, “Since
22654 I have put on the lion's skin, I must not be
22655 faint of heart”--Kratylos, 411A. The tale
[Page326-a+ 50]
22656 appears in the Chinese Avadānas, vol. ii., p.
22657 59, no. 91. Compare also</p>

22682 <p>{@3.@} SELECTION XII. The elephant, the

22720 <p>{@1-2.@} The moon-god's message goes from

22726 <p>{@3.@} See {@prasiddhi,@} and cf. {@śaśa, śaśāṅka,@}
22727 and {@śaśin.@} The Jātaka tells how the rabbit
22728 offered up its own life for Śakra and how
22729 Śakra in grateful recognition placed the
22730 hare's image in the moon's disk as an en-
22731 during memorial of the self-sacrifice--see

22751 <p>{@12.@} SELECTION XIII. The blue jackal.
22752 Hitopadeśa, book iii., fable 7.</p>
22753 <p>--This story corresponds to Pañcatantra,
[Page327-a+ 49]
22754 book i., fable 10. It is not found in the
22755 Arabic version nor in Somadeva, and so was
22756 probably not a part of the Indian original--
22757 Benfey, i. p. 223. But cf. Jātaka, no. 188.
22758 The {%motif%} is similar to that of the fable of

23061 <p>{@11.@} SELECTION XIX. The Brahman and
23062 his jar. Hitopadeśa, book iv., fable 8. Count
23063 not your chickens before they be hatched.</p>
23064 <p>--This story corresponds to Pañcatantra,
23065 book v., fable 9. The history and literature
23066 of the fable are treated at length by Max
23067 Müller, {%Chips,%} iv. 145-209.</p>
23068 <p>--The tale recurs in the {%Arabian Nights,%}
23069 Night 716 (Weil, iii. 910): and the Barber's
23070 story of his fifth brother, Night 166 (Weil,
23071 i. 540: Lane, chap. V.) is essentially similar.
23072 Ultimately dependent on the Indian original
23073 are Grimm's Lazy Heinz and Haggard Liese,
23074 {%Märchen,%} no's 164 and 168. Compare also</p>
23075 <p>Old Syriac version. Kalilag, p. 53.</p>
23076 <p>Arabic. Kalila. Knatchbull, p. 269. Wolff, ii. 3.</p>
23077 <p>Later Syriac version, p. 170.</p>
23078 <p>Anvār-i Suhailī, book vi., story 2.</p>
23079 <p>Directorium, Cap. VII., p. 7 of signature K.</p>
23080 <p>Buch der Beispiele, p. 130^14^.</p>
23081 <p>Benfey, i. 499; ii. 345. Lancereau, p. 345.</p>
23082 <p>La Fontaine, b'k vii., fable 10. Regnier, ii. 145, 495.</p>
23083 <p>--For {@-śarman@} in names, see 59^13^ and N.,
23084 and see {@viṣṇuśarman@} in vocab.</p>
23085 <p>{@11-12.@} The feasts of the winter and
23086 summer solstices (corresponding to Christmas
23087 and Midsummer), originated in the worship
23088 of the sun at his ‘entrance’ ({@saṃkrānti@})
23089 upon his ‘north-course’ and his ‘south-
23090 course’ respectively, and were celebrated
23091 with lavish alms-giving, as appears from the
23092 Pañcatantra, book ii., fable 2 (where Kose-
23093 garten, 119^1^, reads {@uttarāyaṇa-,@} and the
23094 Bombay ed., 14^21^, reads {@dakṣiṇāyana-:@} see
23095 this last in vocab.).</p>
23096 <p>--The vernal equinox was also the occa-
23097 sion of great festivities, whence, doubtless,
23098 the fulness of the Brahman's jar. This feast
23099 survives as the {%Holī%} or Indian Carnival--
23100 described by H. H. Wilson, {%Works,%} ii. 222-43.</p>
[Page330-b+ 49]
23101 <p>{@12-14.@} U. f. {@tatas tam (= śarāvam) ā-dāya,@}
23102 {@asau (devaśarmā), śayyā-nikṣipta + dehas@}
23103 (1297) san (redundant, {@√1as3@}), {@rātrau acin-@}
23104 {@tayat.@} --The long cpd: ‘in a dish-filled-
23105 pot-maker's-shed-part ({@ekadeśe@}).’</p>
23106 <p>{@14-17.@} Construe: {@yadi aham, imam…@}
23107 {@vikrīya,…prāpnomi, tadā@} (introduces verb
23108 of apodosis, {@karomi@}) {@tais (= kapardakais)@}
23109 {@…śarāvān tatas ghaṭa_ādīn upakrīya@}
23110 {@vikrīya, anekadhā vṛddhais…-ādikam@}
23111 {@upakrīya,…utpādya,…karomi.@} As a
23112 help to the correct phrasing, note that the
23113 interjected adverbial clauses end each with
23114 its gerund. {@--tatas,@} line 15, ‘then,’ equiv.

23219 <head>SELECTIONS XXII.-XXVII.</head>
23220 <head>EXTRACTS FROM THE KATHĀ-SARIT-SĀGARA.</head>
23221 <p>§ 45. The work from which the following extracts are taken holds a rather
23222 exceptional place in Hindu literary history, inasmuch as its date and authorship are
23223 quite definitely known. According to the author's fancy, it unites in itself all
23224 stories as does the ocean all rivers, and he therefore calls it the ‘Ocean of the
23225 Streams of Story’ or Kathā-sarit-sāgara. Following out the metaphor, he divides
23226 the work into one hundred and twenty-four chapters, called {@taraṅgas@} or ‘billows.’
23227 By another division, independent of the one just mentioned, the work is broken into
23228 eighteen books, called {@lambakas,@} which Brockhaus, without authority, conjectured
23229 to mean ‘surges.’ The work contains about 22,000 distichs, that is, about one-
[Page332-a+ 68]
23230 quarter as much as the Mahā-bhārata, and not quite twice as much as the Iliad and
23231 Odyssey together. An analysis of its contents is given by II. II. Wilson, {%Works,%}
23232 vol. iv. 109-159.</p>
23233 <p>§ 46. BIBLIOGRAPHY. The following discussions connected with the literary
23234 history of this work should be mentioned.</p>
23235 <p>Fitzedward Hail, the Vāsavadattā, Calcutta, 1859,
23236 Introduction, pages 22, 23.</p>
23237 <p>George Bühler, On the Vṛihatkathā of Kshemen-
23238 dra, {%Indian Antiquary,%} i. 302f, Bombay, 1872. Cf.
23239 Weber's Remarks, ibidem, ii. 57.</p>
23240 <p>George Bühler, Detailed Report of a tour in search
23241 of Sanskrit MSS. made [in 1875] in Kaśmīr etc., pub-
23242 lished at Bombay in 1877 as an Extra Number of the
23243 {%Journal%} of the Bombay Branch of the Royal Asiatic
23244 Society, with vol. xii. Preliminary reporta had ap-
23245 peared in the {%Ind. Ant.,%} v. 27f, and vi. 264f.</p>
23246 <p>George Bühler, Ueber das Zeitalter des Kaśmi-
23247 rischen Dichters Somadeva, {%Sitzungsberichte%} der
23248 phii. hist. Classe der Kais. Akademie der Wiss.,
23249 cx. 545f, Vienaa, 1885.</p>
23250 <p>Sylvain Lévi, La Bṛihatkathāmañjari de Kshe-
23251 mendra, {%Journal Asiatique,%} 8. vi. 397f, vii. 178f. Vol.
23252 vi. contains chapters 1-8 in text and translation.</p>
23253 <p>The text has been edited by H. Brockhaus,
23254 Leipsic, 1839, 1862, and 1866. A complete trans-
23255 lation has been given by C. H. Tawney, Calcutta,
23256 1880-84.</p>
23257 <p>§ 47. The concluding verses of the work were printed from new and trust worthy
23258 manuscript material by Bühler.^1^ From them it appears that the author was named
23259 Soma, i. e. Somadeva, and was the son of the virtuous Brahman Rāma. Somadeva
23260 says that he made this collection of stories to please the queen Sūryavatī, and gives
23261 some of the facts relating to the royal house of Kaśmīr then regnant. These facts
23262 are supplemented and confirmed by the Rājataraṅginī or ‘Chronicles of Kaśmīr,’
23263 written by Kalhaṇa, about 1148-1157 A. D.^2^ Combining these sources, Bühler
23264 reaches conclusions which may here be summarized briefly.</p>
23265 <p>§ 48. In the year 79, i. e. 4079, of {@loka-kāla,^3^@} Saṃgrāmarāja, a descendant of
23266 Sātavāhana (of Śāta-), succeeded to the throne of Kaśmīr. This was A. D. 1003. He
23267 was followed in 1028 by his son Ananta. Anantadeva is described as weak-minded,
23268 rash, and impetuous, but as brave even to fool-hardiness. He married Sūryavatī,
23269 daughter of the king of Jālandhar ({@jālaṃdhara@}). She brought him entirely under
23270 her power, and induced him, in 1063, to abdicate in favor of his son Kalaśadeva.
23271 He soon repented his hasty step and got control of the government again. Mean-
23272 time, Kalaśa abandoned himself to every vice, and, in rage, his parents determined
23273 to punish him, and give over the kingdom to his eldest son Harṣa. In fact, Ananta
23274 retired with his court to Vijayakṣetra, the modern Bījbahār, in [41]55 = A. D. 1079,
23275 and after two years of feud with Kalaśa, took his own life in 1081, leaving the power
23276 in the hands of Kalaśa, who held it till 1089, when he was succeeded by Harṣa.
23277 Sūryavatī burned herself on the funeral pile of her husband Ananta. Now since,
23278 according to Somadeva, Kalaśa was king when the work was finished, and since it
23279 was written for Sūryavatī, the date of the composition must fall between Ananta's
23280 first abdication and his wife's death, i. e. between 1063 and 1081 A. D.</p>
23281 <p>§ 49. The real source of the Kathā-sarit-sāgara is stated by Somadeva^4^ to be
23282 the Bṛhat-kathā or ‘Great Narration’ of Guṇāḍhya. This work, according to
23283 Bühler,^5^ goes back to the first or second century of the Christian era; but no manu-
23284 script of it has yet been published. On the other hand, important evidence of its
23285 general character and contents is afforded by the two works that have flowed from
23286 <note n="332-1"><p>{%Ueber das Zeitalter%} etc., pages 547-549.</p></note>
23287 <note n="332-2"><p>Bühler's {%Report,%} p. 52f; {%Zeitalter,%} p. 554, 557.</p></note>
23288 <note n="332-3"><p>The popular calendar ({%loka-kāla%}) of Kaśmīr
23289 is counted from a date corresponding to 3076 B. C.
23290 The era is connected--in some real or fanciful way
23291 --with Ursa Major ({%saptarṣayas%}--see {%ṛṣi%}2), and so
23292 is called the Saptarshi era. It is still in use. In giv-
23293 ing a date, the hundreds and thousands are usually
23294 omitted. See {%Report,%} pages 59-60.</p></note>
23295 <note n="332-4"><p>See Kathā-sarit-sāgara, i. 3. and cxxiv. 250, and
23296 Reader 56^5^.</p></note>
23297 <note n="332-5"><p>{%Report,%} p. 47. Cf. Wilson, l. c., p. 119f.</p></note>
[Page333-a+ 56]
23298 it, the Bṛhat-kathā-mañjarī and the Kathā-sarit-sāgara. Each is a recast of Guṇ-
23299 āḍhya's original. The former was made by Kṣemendra Vyāsa-dāsa, a contemporary
23300 of Somadeva. Its date is not far from the year 1037 A. D., and it is therefore some
23301 thirty or forty years prior to Somadeva's work.^1^ Both Kṣemendra and Somadeva
23302 distinctly assert that they translated from an original in the {@paiśācī bhāṣā@} or
23303 ‘Goblin dialect’; and internal evidence^2^ confirms their assertions, and, further,
23304 makes it certain that each worked independently of the other. But Somadeva seems
23305 to have been well aware of the faults of his predecessor, and his work is a decided
23306 improvement upon that of Kṣemendra, whose recast is only about one-third as long
23307 as Somadeva's, and sacrifices poetic merit in the effort to be brief.</p>
23308 <p>§ 50. Although Somadeva was a Brahman, there are yet many remaining traces
23309 of the Buddhist character of his original, and even direct allusions^3^ to the Buddhist
23310 Birth Stories occur. Indeed, Weber maintained^4^ that Guṇāḍhya was a Buddhist.
23311 The name {@paiśācī bhāṣā@} seems to have covered a number of Low Prākrit dialects^5^
23312 spoken by the most ignorant and degraded classes in many different parts of India.
23313 In one of these dialects the Bṛhat-kathā is actually written.</p>
23314 <p>§ 51. It remains--partly by way of introduction to selection xxvii.--to com-
23315 plete and summarize the half-mythical account there given of the origin of the stories.</p>
23316 <p>In a retired spot on the Himālayan peak Kailāsa, the god Śiva, to please his
23317 wife Pārvatī, was telling her (Kathā-sarit-sāgara, i. 48) the adventures of the Seven
23318 Fairy Kings. Unfortunately, he was overheard by his Gaṇa or attendant (see {@gaṇa@}),
23319 Puṣpadanta. Puṣpadanta foolishly told the tale to his wife, and she recited it in
23320 turn to Pārvatī. This made it appear that Śiva had palmed off upon her an old
23321 story. Even on learning the truth, she was still exceedingly enraged, and cursed
23322 Puṣpadanta to be born as a mortal. She also inflicted the same curse on the Gaṇa
23323 Mālyavant who interceded for him.</p>
23324 <p>At their entreaty, however, she set a limit ({@anta@}) to the curse of each, speaking
23325 (i. 59) to this effect: “There is a Yakṣa named Supratīka, who, cursed to become a
23326 mortal, is living in the Vindhya as a Piśāca, under the name of Kāṇabhūti. When
23327 thou, Puṣpadanta, shalt see him, and tell him the tale which thou overheardest Śiva
23328 telling me, then thou shalt be released from thy curse. When Kāṇabhūti shall have
23329 told it to thee, Mālyavant, he shall be released. And when thou, Mālyavant, shalt
23330 have published it to the world, thou shalt be free also.”</p>
23331 <p>§ 52. Puṣpadanta, accordingly, is born as Vararuci or Kātyāyana, meets Kāṇa-
23332 bhūti, narrates to him the seven stories of adventure in seven hundred thousand
23333 stanzas (ii. 26), and is released.</p>
23334 <p>Kāṇabhūti, meantime, remained in the Vindhya, waiting the coming of Mālya-
23335 vant. The latter is duly born as a Brahman (vi. 19, 20), with the name Guṇāḍhya,
23336 at Supratiṣṭhita (vi. 8) in Pratiṣṭhāna.^6^ This is identified with Paiṭhān on the
23337 Godāvarī (vi. 72), about 150 miles from Bombay, east by north. After travelling in
23338 the Deccan, Guṇāḍhya returns to the splendid royal city of Supratiṣṭhita (vi. 24) and
23339 is appointed a minister (vi. 70) of Sātavāhana, the king of whom the story in selec-
23340 <note n="333-1"><p>Lévi, {%Journ. As.,%} 8. vii. 218-220.</p></note>
23341 <note n="333-2"><p>{%Ind. Ant.,%} i. 308-309. {%Journ. AS.,%} 8. vi. 413.</p></note>
23342 <note n="333-3"><p>E. g., at lxxii. 120, to the Boar Jātaka. See Weber,
23343 {%Indische Streifen,%} ii. 367.</p></note>
23344 <note n="333-4"><p>{%Ind. Ant.,%} ii. 57.</p></note>
23345 <note n="333-5"><p>For the views of the Hindus and others concern-
23346 ing these dialects, see Muir, {%Orig. Sanskrit Texts,%}
23347 ii^3^. 43f, esp. p. 48 and 50. Cf. also Jacobi, Ausgewählte
23348 Erzählungen in Māhārāshṭrī, Einl. § 2, § 15.</p></note>
23349 <note n="333-6"><p>Its Prākrit form is Païtthāna; this is the HAI-
23350 ©ANA of the <gr></gr> and the <gr></gr> of Ptolemy.
23351 See McCrindle, {%Ancient India as described by Ptol-%}
23352 {%emy,%} pages 175-6, and J. Burgess, {%Arch. Survey of%}
23353 {%Western India,%} iii. (Bīdar, etc.) p. 55.</p></note>
[Page334-a+ 54]
23354 tion xxvi. is related. In consequence of a wager with his colleague Śarvavarman
23355 (52^1^), Guṇāḍhya forswears the use of Sanskrit, Prākrit, and the local veruacular.
23356 Since he knows no other language, he is forced to keep silence, and so to give up his
23357 position as minister. With two pupils he retires to the Vindhya, and, falling in
23358 with a host of Piśācas (vii. 26), learns their dialect. In this he addresses Kāṇabhūti,
23359 who exists himself at the time as a Piśāca. Kāṇabhūti tells the tales in ‘his own
23360 dialect’ ({@svabhāṣayā,@} 53^4^), i. e. Paiśācī, to Guṇāḍhya. The hearer writes them down
23361 in Paiśācī, and with his own blood, since he had no ink by him. Kāṇabhūti is then
23362 released.</p>
23363 <p>Guṇāḍhya, in order to “publish the stories to the world,” sends the manuscript
23364 to Sātavāhana. The king, puffed up by his newly-acquired knowledge of Sanskrit
23365 grammar (52^28^), rejects the stories on account of their dialect. Guṇāḍhya, overcome
23366 with grief, reads aloud to his two pupils six hundred thousand distichs and casts the
23367 manuscript, leaf by leaf, into the fire, while the beasts stand about in a circle and
23368 listen with tears in their eyes.^1^ One hundred thousand couplets Guṇāḍhya keeps,
23369 because they especially pleased his pupils. At last the king hears of the strange
23370 proceeding, comes, and takes what is left,^1^ namely the Bṛhat-kathā. The pupils
23371 expounded the poem to the king and he composed the first book or {@lambaka,@} called
23372 Kathāpīṭha (56^10^N.), to serve as a general introduction to the rest, after Guṇāḍhya
23373 had ascended to heaven (56^4^), released.^2^</p>
23374 <note n="334-1"><p>Compare the atoriea of Orpheus and of the
23375 Sibyl.</p></note>
23376 <note n="334-2"><p>According, then, to Somadeva (and to Kṣemen-
23377 dra, as well, {%Ind. Ant.,%} i. 307), the <gr></gr> of the
23378 stories is, in turn, from</p></note>
23379 <note n="334-1."><p>Śiva, to</p></note>
23380 <note n="334-2."><p>Puṣpadanta (= Vararuci, Kātyāyana),</p></note>
23381 <note n="334-3."><p>Supratīka (= Kāṇabhūti),</p></note>
23382 <note n="334-4."><p>Mālyavant (= Guṇāḍhya),</p></note>
23383 <note n="334-5."><p>Sātavāhana, and</p></note>
23384 <note n="334-6."><p>The world.</p></note>

23431 <p>{@1.@} SELECTION XXIII. Story of Mousey,
23432 the thrifty merchant. Kathā-sarit-sāgara,
23433 Taraṅga, vi. 28.--It is introduced as a story
23434 which Guṇāḍhya hears on his return to
23435 Supratiṣṭhita--see p. 333, § 52. It may be

23437 It is identical with the Cullaka-seṭṭhi Jātaka,
23438 no. 4 (vol. i.), transl., i. p. 168.</p>
23439 <p>{@2.@} ‘By me, utterly without ({@vinā eva@})
23440 capital, wealth was gained’ ({@√sad + ā,@} caus.).</p>
23441 <p>{@4. man-mātus,@} abl., ‘from my mother:’

23496 <p>{@1.@} SELECTION XXIV. King Śibi, the fal-
23497 con, and the dove. Kathā-sarit-sāgara, Tar.
23498 vii. 88.--This story is famous, old, and wide-
23499 spread. It is of distinctly Buddhistic origin
23500 and character--see S. Beal, {%Si-yu-ki,%} i. p. 125
23501 and note. Benfey, Pañcatantra, i. p. 388 f,
23502 gives a great many Buddhist and other
23503 parallels. It occurs in the Jātaka as no. 499
23504 (vol. iv.). It is frequently figured on Bud-
23505 dhist sculptures: so on the tope of Amarāva-
23506 tī--see James Fergusson, {%Tree and Serpent%}
23507 {%Worship,^2^%} plate lxxxiii. 1, and p. 225, and
23508 plate lx. left, and p. 194; also on the great
23509 Javan temple of Bōrō Boudour. The Ama-
23510 rāvatī tope dates from about the beginning
23511 of our era--see J. Burgess, {%Arch. Survey of%}
23512 {%Southern India%} (Amarāvatī, etc.), p. 12, 101.</p>
23513 <p>--In the Cariyā-piṭaka, Pāli text, ed.
23514 Morris, p. 77, Śibi appears as an incarnation
23515 of the Future Buddha. Sakka, in the form
23516 of a blind old beggar, asks him for one of
23517 his eyes, and he gladly gives up both of
23518 them. This is represented in a mural paint-
23519 ing of a cave at Ajaṇṭā--J. Burgess, {%Cave%}
23520 {%Temples of India,%} p. 315. Beal gives a
23521 Chinese version of the story in his {%Buddhist%}
23522 {%Literature in China,%} p. 31-41. And the tale
23523 occurs in Mohammedan forms with Moses
23524 in place of Śibi, and Michael and Gabriel in
23525 place of Indra and Dharma (or Agni).</p>
23526 <p>--In Sanskrit works the Śibi-story is
23527 common. We find it in the Southern Pañ-
[Page336-a+ 49]
23528 catantra, French version by J.-A. Dubois,
23529 p. 173. It occurs three times in the MBh.
23530 It is told at iii. 130. 21 = 10557, of Uśīnara,
23531 the father of Śibi (translated by P. E. Fou-
23532 caux, {%Le MBh., Onze épisodes,%} p. 231); at
23533 iii. 197. 1 = 13274, of Śibi himself; and at
23534 xiii. 32. 3 = 2046, of Vṛṣadarbha, the son of
23535 Śibi.</p>
23536 <p>--Śibi Auśīnara is the traditional author
23537 of RV. x. 179. 1, and seems to be the heros
23538 eponymos of a clan that dwelt between the
23539 Indus and Akesines. See Zimmer, {%Altin-%}
23540 {%disches Leben,%} p. 130, 431.</p>
23541 <p>{@1.@} King Suśarman, having been deceived
23542 by a Gaṇa in the form of a Brahman, says,
23543 “This is no Brahman, but a god come to
23544 deceive me; for such things are constantly
23545 happening in this world; and so ({@tathā ca@}--
23546 introducing the following story as a similar
23547 instance), in old times, there was ({@abhūt@}) a
23548 king, etc.”</p>

23576 <p>{@20.@} SELECTION XXV. Story of Ahalyā.
[Page336-b+ 48]
23577 Kathā-sarit-sāgara, Tar. xvii. 137.--Śacī is a
23578 pattern of wifely virtue, but Indra does not
23579 requite her with equal fidelity. His amours
23580 are as notorious as those of Zeus. Indra is
23581 invoked with the words {@ahalyāyai jāra,@} as
23582 early as the ŚB. (iii. 3. 4^18^). He is reproached
23583 for his affair with Ahalyā, at MBh. v. 12. 6 =
23584 373; and this is narrated at length in the
23585 Rāmāyaṇa, i. 48 Schlegel, or i. 49 Gorresio.
23586 His attempt upon Ruci is told at MBh. xiii.
23587 40. 16 = 2262, excerpted by Muir, {%Texts,%} i^2^. 466.
23588 Later books smooth over these immoralities
23589 by interpreting them allegorically--see
23590 Müller, {%Ancient Skt. Lit.,%} p. 529.--So Holtz-
23591 mann, ZDMG. xxxii. 302 (cf. 294); Muir,
23592 {%Texts,%} iv^2^. 48.</p>

23623 <p>{@16.@} SELECTION XXVI. The king who
23624 didn't know his Sanskrit grammar. Kathā-
[Page337-a+ 49]
23625 sarit-sāgara, Tar. vi. 108-164, omitting 111-
23626 112, 124-133a, 154-158, 161-162.--Kṣemen-

23744 <p>{@1-2.@} U. f. {@prādus āsan ca tās@} (sciences)

23746 <p>{@3.@} SELECTION XXVII. The pathetic his-
23747 tory of the stories. Kathā-sarit-sāgara, Tar.
23748 viii.--It forms Kṣemendra's eighth chapter
23749 (of only 16 couplets), {%Jour. As.,%} 8. vi. 449.
23750 For a general explanation of the Taraṅga,
23751 see p. 333, § 51f.</p>
23752 <p>{@3-4.@} ‘So, by G's request (vii. 113), that
23753 tale was told by K. in his own ({@piśāca@})
23754 dialect.’</p>
23755 <p>{@5-6.@} ‘And by G., likewise in (lit. with)
23756 that dialect, in (lit. with, 281c) seven years
23757 ({@varṣais@}), it ({@sā@}) was written down as seven
23758 couplet-lacs’ (appositively).</p>
23759 <p>{@7-8.@} U. f. {@mā@} (580) {@…hārṣus@} (882) {@iti:@}
23760 ‘Thinking “Let them not steal it,” i. e. Fear-
23761 ing lest they might.’</p>
23762 <p>--Both Somadeva and Kṣemendra state
23763 that the stories were written down in blood:
23764 S. gives the lack of ink as a reason; K.
23765 gives none. But the statement may rest on
23766 a popular superstition like the Germanic
23767 one which ascribes peculiar virtues to pure
23768 spittle (cf. also St. Mark viii. 23) and to
23769 blood. Life rests in the blood, the “vital
[Page338-b+ 49]
23770 fluid”; from it a drink of immortality is
23771 made; and with it pacts are made or sub-
23772 scribed (cf. Faust, 1. 1383).</p>
23773 <p>--At present (1878), all available informa-
23774 tion points to a Phoenician-Aramaic origin
23775 of the Indian alphabets. Writing was prob-
23776 ably introduced not earlier than 400 B. C.,
23777 and was, certainly, little used in India before
23778 250 B. C. So says Burnell, {%Palaeography,^2^%} p. 9.
23779 See also Whitney, § 2. The material was
23780 the palm-leaf, and in Kaśmīr, birch-bark.
23781 Paper was introduced by the Mohammedans,
23782 i. e. not till after 1000 A. D. For ink, see
23783 {@maṣī@} in vocab.</p>
23784 <p>{@9-10.@} ‘The sky ({@ambaram@}) became filled
23785 ({@nirantaram@}) with S. etc., who came ({@āyā-@}
23786 {@tais@}) to listen, [became filled {%or%} covered] as
23787 if ({@iva@}) with a canopy.’</p>
23788 <p>{@12.@} See {@√gam4.@} K. was orig. a {@yakṣa,@}
23789 see p. 333, § 51. {@--śāpa-muktas@} would be
23790 better.</p>
23791 <p>{@15-16.@} ‘I must make my B. famous on
23792 earth; and this business (of making it so)
23793 was enjoined on (lit. announced to, {@ud-īritas@})
23794 me by Durgā when she told how my curse
23795 would end (lit. in the declaration, {@-uktau,@} of
23796 the end, {@-anta-@}).’ See p. 333, § 51.</p>
23797 <p>{@19-20.@} The two nominatives in 1. 19 are
23798 in partitive apposition to {@śiṣyau…ubhau,@}
23799 the subject of {@ūcatus.@}</p>

23805 <p>{@7-8.@} ‘That MS. was shown to S. (geni-

23807 work.”’ --The gender of the pred. deter-
23808 mines that of the subject, {@eṣā.@}</p>
23809 <p>{@11.@} See {@pramāṇa.@} “The length of the
23810 poem (700,000 distichs) is a weighty argu-
23811 ment in its favor, but--” This is quite in
23812 accord with later Hindu taste. As if the

23816 couplets for the use of the gods. Fortu-
23817 nately their years were lengthened out so as
23818 to give them plenty of time to hear it--see
[Page339-a+ 45]
