26292 <p>SELECTION XL. RV. v. 40. Indra and
26293 Atri, and the sun eclipsed by the demon.--
26294 For a critical analysis of the hymn, see
26295 Grassmann, Translation, i. 190 and esp. 540.
26296 Stanzas 6, 7, 8, here given, are quite inde-
26297 pendent of the rest.</p>
26298 <p>--Ludwig identifies the eclipse here re-
26299 ferred to with that of April 20, 1001 B. C.,
26300 O. S.--see {%Sitzungsberichte d. böhmischen%}
26301 {%Gesell. d. Wiss.,%} 1885. His argument is
26302 reported by Whitney, JAOS. xiii. p. lxi f =
26303 PAOS. Oct. 1885, and by Bergaigne, JA.
26304 8. vi. 372f. No fair interpretation of the text
26305 furnishes data precise enough for an exact
26306 identification.</p>
26307 <p>--Stanzas 6 and 8 are spoken by the poet;
26308 stanza 7, by the Sun. The Sun's foe, Súar-
26309 bhānu (accent !), is ‘he who has the sun's
26310 beams,’ and is later identified with Rāhu,
26311 the demon who ‘swallows,’ ‘devours,’ or
26312 ‘seizes’ ({@√2gṛ, √gras@}--cf. 23^1^N., {@√grah@}) the
26313 sun or moon, and so causes eclipses. The
26314 Sun is in terror, and implores Atri for help:
26315 and the latter rescues the orb by prayer and
26316 praise.</p>
26317 <p>--Atri's mythical exploit is often men-
26318 tioned in the Brāhmaṇas (see texts in Lud-
[Page369-b+ 49]
26319 wig, v. 508; and cf. SBE. xxvi. 346). Thus
26320 the PB., at vi. 6. 8, 11, narrates how, at the
26321 request of the gods, Atri restored to bright-
26322 ness the sun, which the demon had smitten
26323 with darkness. On this account, it continues,
26324 a present of gold, the symbol of brightness,
26325 is made to a descendant of Atri at a sacrifice
26326 now-a-days.</p>
26327 <p>--Interesting are the Greek ideas about
26328 the <gr></gr> that bringeth to nought
26329 men's strength and wisdom--see Pindar's
26330 {%Fragments%} [74], Bergk^4^ i. 411f and notes.</p>
26331 <p>{@14. ava_áhan@} (593), cf. 70^7^ and note.</p>
26332 <p>{@15. gūḻhám, √guh. --ápa-vrata,@} ‘having
26333 action off, i. e. baffling,’ or, as in vocab. --
26334 ‘The sun, hidden…, Atri found ({@avindat@})
26335 by the fourth prayer.’</p>
26336 <p>--Ludwig reasons thus: The other priests
26337 had a regular liturgy of three prayers for
26338 combating eclipses. On this occasion, these
26339 proved ineffectual. Atri knew a fourth, and
26340 that brought the sun out. Hence the totality
26341 must have been very long. This matter is
26342 discussed in full by Whitney, l. c., p. lxv (= xxi).
26343 Bergaigne, l. c., p. 383 (= 14), says it is a
26344 mere case of the use of a sacred number
26345 plus one--cf. {%Rel. védique,%} ii. 128.</p>
26346 <p>{@16. mā́m@} (pronounce {@mā́am@}) {@imám,@} ‘me
26347 here, i. e. in this plight;’ Sāyaṇa, {@īdṛg-avas-@}
26348 {@tham.@} --U. f. {@atre:@} the Sun is addressing
26349 Atri. {@--irasyā́,@} 365. 1. {@--bhiyás-ā,@} ‘(me,
26350 who am thine) with fear, i. e. who am thine,
26351 (but) sore in terror:’ or, the word {%may%} be
26352 taken actively, as the accent (1151. 2c) and
26353 Sāyaṇa's gloss (‘fear-inspiring’) suggest,
26354 and joined with the subject--‘with a
26355 frightening.’ {@--mā́…ní gārīt: √2gṛ;@} aor.,
26356 899a; combination w. {@mā́,@} 579.</p>
26357 <p>{@17.@} Pronounce {@tuám mitṛó asi…taú@}
26358 {@mehā́vatam. --mitró,@} ‘friend.’ --U. f. {@taú@}
26359 (see {@tá2@} end) {@mā ihá avatam:@} ‘so do ye
26360 two help me here, (thou) and V.’ With the
26361 peculiar omission of {@tvám@} compare that of
26362 {@ahám,@} 79^10^N. So RV. viii. 1. 6.</p>
26363 <p>{@18@} and 77^1^. {@brahmā́ = átris@} of next line.
26364 {@--yuyujānás@} (807), see {@√yuj2. --kīrí@} may
26365 rather mean ‘praise.’ {@--upaśíkṣan,@} see
26366 {@√śak + upa.@} --U. f. {@cákṣus ā́_adhāt@} (830).
26367 {@--ápa aghukṣat, √guh,@} 920, 916.</p>

26368 <head>NOTES TO PAGE {@77.@}</head>
26369 <p>SELECTION XLI. RV. vii. 55. Magic
26370 spells to produce sleep.--Ascribed to Vas-
26371 iṣṭha. The hymn consists of three parts
26372 differing in metre and disconnected in con-
26373 tents. For a general discussion of it, see
26374 Colebrooke's {%Essays%} ^2^i. 27, and Whitney's
26375 note, ibid. p. 112; JAOS. iii. 336f; and esp.
26376 Aufrecht, ISt. iv. 337f. Sāyaṇa reports the
26377 native traditions about the hymn. Thus:</p>
26378 <p>Vasiṣṭha came by night to the house of
26379 Varuṇa--to sleep, say some; to steal grain,
26380 say others. He was assailed by the watch-
26381 dog, which bayed at him, and fain would
26382 have bitten him; but he laid the hound
26383 asleep with the stanza beginning,
26384 When, silv'ry Sārameya, thou,
26385 and the one following.--Later superstition
26386 uses the hymn to quiet uncanny creatures
26387 at night: see Rigvidhāna, ii. 26.</p>
26388 <p>--FIRST PART. The hymn next preceding
26389 this in the RV., vii. 54, is to Vāstoṣpati, the
26390 Lar familiaris, and consists of three stanzas.
26391 The first part of our hymn, vii. 55. 1, is a mere
26392 addition to these three stanzas, and belongs
26393 accordingly to vii. 54 rather than to vii. 55,
26394 as appears from the fact that the four
26395 stanzas are all recited together in the cere-
26396 mony of moving into a new house. See
26397 ŚGS. iii. 4, AGS. ii. 9. 9, and esp. PGS. iii. 4. 7
26398 --all in SBE. xxix.; and cf. MS. i. 5. 13.</p>
26399 <p>The joining of this stanza to hymn 55 is a
26400 simple misdivision of which the NT. shows
26401 many parallel instances. Thus Mark ix. 1
26402 belongs to viii.; chap. ix. should begin with
26403 the Transfiguration. A converse misdivision
26404 is at AV. vi. 63-64 as compared with RV.
26405 x. 191.</p>
26406 <p>--SECOND PART. Stanzas 2-4 are part of
26407 a scene at the entrance to Yama's kingdom
26408 or the regions of the blessed. Yama's two
26409 watch-dogs (cf. 83^17^N. and introd.) guard the
26410 pathway and keep out the wicked. Here
26411 one of them barks at some who would come
26412 in; and these, in turn, protest that they
26413 are godly men, and so have a right to
26414 enter in peace. See Kaegi, N. 274; Zimmer,
26415 p. 421.</p>
[Page370-b+ 50]
26416 <p>--THIRD PART. Stanzas 5-8 are the in-
26417 cantations by which the entire household is
26418 put to sleep. According to Aufrecht, l. c.
26419 (or Zimmer, p. 308), it is while a maid is
26420 receiving the visit of her lover; but that
26421 seems a little doubtful because we have
26422 plurals {@nas@} and {@sáṃ hanmas@} and {@vayám,@}
26423 lines 12, 13, 15, and not duals.</p>
26424 <p>With stanzas 5, 6, 7, 8 correspond respec-
26425 tively stanzas 6, 5, 1, 3 of AV. iv. 5, and the
26426 variants are interesting. The third part has
26427 nothing to do with the second; but the dia-
26428 skeuasts have juxtaposed them because in
26429 both a dog is put to sleep.</p>

26479 <p>SELECTION XLII. RV. vii. 56. To the
26480 Maruts or gods of the storm-winds.--Com-
26481 pare selection xxxv. (73^4^) and introduction.
26482 The hymn consists of two parts, of which
26483 the first, here given, ends with stanza 10.
26484 Number 11 is only the fragment of a stanza.
26485 The rest (12-25) is in an entirely different
26486 metre.</p>
26487 <p>--The metre of the ten stanzas is without
26488 doubt a secondary one, being simply a form
26489 of the {@triṣṭubh@} syncopated at the sixth place,
26490 i. e. with the prevailingly light sixth syllable
26491 left out (cf. JAOS. xi. p. lxiii = PAOS. May,
26492 1881). Thus,
26493 <gr></gr>.
26494 Each of the parts into which the verse is
26495 broken is a ‘syllable-pentad’ or {@akṣara-@}
26496 {@paṅkti;@} and each is an independent {@pāda,@}
26497 since verbs at the beginning of the even
26498 pentads are accented, e. g., at vii. 34. 3^b^, 3^d^, 4^b^,
26499 6^b^. A pentad-couplet is sometimes joined
26500 with a simply broken but unsyncopated
26501 {@triṣṭubh@} verse to form a half-stanza or
26502 stanza (e. g. i. 67. 8^cd^; 70. 4^cd^, 10^cd^; x. 46. 1^gh^);
26503 and this fact seems to suggest the derivative
26504 character of the pentads.</p>
26505 <p>--The {@gāyatrī@}-stanza has 3 verses of 8
26506 syllables; and the strophe has 3 stanzas, a
26507 multiple of the number of verses. The
26508 pentad-stanza has 4 or 8 verses of 5 syl-
26509 lables; and the hymn has 5, 10, or 20 stan-
26510 zas, a multiple of the number of syllables.
26511 The hymns RV. i. 65-70 have each 5 stanzas
26512 of 8 pentasyllabic verses; while vii. 34A and
26513 vii. 56A and ix. 109 probably consisted origi-
[Page371-b+ 49]
26514 nally each of 10 or 20 stanzas of 4 penta-
26515 syllabic verses. Compare Bollensen, ZDMG.
26516 xxii. 572f.</p>
26517 <p>{@18.@} U. f. {@ké īm víaktās…ádha suáśvās.@}</p>
26518 <head>NOTES TO PAGE {@78.@}</head>
26519 <p>{@1.@} U. f. {@nákis hí eṣām:@} cf. John iii. 8, <gr></gr>
26520 <gr></gr> {@--vidre,@}
26521 790b, 798a.</p>
26522 <p>{@2. sva-pū́bhis,@} ‘with their ({@sva-@}) wings,’
26523 taking {@-pū@} in the sense of {@pávana. Pávana,@}
26524 lit. ‘a cleaner,’ from {@√pū,@} sometimes means

26533 <p>--Here the storm-gods are pictured as
26534 lusty eagles, each sportively striving with
26535 the others for precedence, and spreading his
26536 wings over them in turn, to put them down
26537 and master them.</p>
26538 <p>--Ludwig interprets, ‘They vie in over-
26539 whelming each other with their blasts’
26540 ({@pávana@} has also the mg ‘wind’). This
26541 view accords with the character of the play-
26542 ful, boisterous, and roaring gods, but not
26543 with the conception of them as eagles; and
26544 it would seem to require the reading {@abhí@}
26545 {@…vápantaḥ.@}</p>

26547 that {@svapū́@} is a ‘besom (that raises the dust)’:
26548 ‘they bestrew each other (in sport) with
26549 dust.’ At best, the line is doubtful.</p>

26756 <p>SELECTION XLV. RV. vii. 89. To Va-
26757 ruṇa.--The hymn has been often translated:

26761 <p>--It is the prayer of a man who is {@varuṇa-@}
26762 {@gṛhīta,@} ‘seized by Varuṇa, i. e. afflicted with
26763 the dropsy.’ V. is god of the waters (cf.
26764 7^10^N., 79^15^N.), and the disease is supposed to
26765 come from him and as a punishment for sin:
26766 see AV. iv. 16. 7; also AB. vii. 15, {@atha@}
26767 {@ha_aikṣvākaṃ varuṇo jagrāha; tasya@}
26768 {@ha_udaraṃ jajñe;@} and ŚB. ii. 5. 2^2^. Cf.
26769 Hillebrandt, p. 63-65, 54. Ludwig's interpre-
26770 tation is quite different: see his notes, vol.

26774 Thorpe's {%Analecta,%} p. 142, entitled {%The Grave,%}
26775 and familiar through Longfellow's transla-
26776 tion. {@--gamam,@} 833, 835: with {@mā́,@} 579.
26777 {@--mṛḻā́,@} 248c, {@√mṛḍ.@}</p>
26778 <p>{@3-4.@} ‘When I go, tottering along, like a
26779 bag, puffed up,--have mercy.’ {@--√sphur@}
26780 implies quick or vigorous motion--see vocab.
26781 Here the iva is not a particle of comparison;
26782 it modifies the mg of the root to that of a
26783 languid shake or wabble such as is character-
26784 istic of a dropsical person. So {@pra-hasan,@}
26785 ‘laughing out;’ {@pra-hasann iva,@} ‘smiling.’</p>
26786 <p>{@--dhmātás,@} ‘puffed up’ (with wind) or
26787 ‘bloated’ (with serum), has reference not
26788 only to the bag, but also to the understood
26789 {@ahám.@} Grassmann, ‘schnaufend,’ i. e. ‘blown,
26790 winded, puffing.’ Otherwise Bergaigne, iii.
26791 155N. {@--adrivas@} (454b), always of Indra,
26792 except here, and at ix. 53. 1 (of Soma) ! --

26882 <p>{@16-17.@} NAMUCI-MYTH. ‘With foam of
26883 the waters, Namuci's head, O Indra, thou
26884 didst cause to fly asunder, when thou wast
26885 conquering all thy foes.’ It appears to me
26886 likely that the natural phenomenon to which
26887 this refers is a water-spout (‘Trombe’) on
26888 an inland lake. This fear-inspiring thing
26889 may well be personified as a demon. The
26890 verb {@úd avartayas@} means ‘didst cause to
26891 move out or fly asunder with a gyratory or
26892 centrifugal motion.’ This accords well with
26893 the facts of the not infrequent phenomenon
26894 as seen by unscientific eyes. See Major
26895 Sherwill on Bengal waterspouts, JASB. 1860,
26896 xxix. 366f, with good pictures, and Th. Reye,
26897 {%Die Wirbelstürme%}^2^, p. 17f. The line MBh.
26898 v. 10. 37 = 328 seems to favor my view. The
26899 whole passage is a reminiscence of the
26900 Namuci-myth.</p>
26901 <p>The head of the column is twisted and
26902 made to burst asunder and scatter itself
26903 ({@phénena,@} instr. of accompaniment, lit. ‘with
26904 foam,’ i. e.) in abundant foamy masses. Then,
26905 with the dispersion of the column, often
[Page375-b+ 49]
26906 comes (Sherwill, 370; Reye, 32) a heavy
26907 rain. All this is set forth as gracious Indra's
26908 prowess. --U. f. {@ájayas spṛ́dhas,@} see 74^12^N.</p>
26909 <p>Bergaigne's discussion, ii. 346-7, may be
26910 compared and also RV. v. 30. 7, 8 and vi. 20. 6.</p>
26911 <p>--The form of the myth as it appears in
26912 the Brāhmaṇas originates in a misconception
26913 of the case-relation of {@phénena@} (see above),
26914 which they take as an instr. of means, i. e.
26915 as the weapon by means of which Indra
26916 slew Namuci. So Sāyaṇa: {@phenena vajrī-@}
26917 {@bhūtena;@} see vocab., {@√sic3.@} Cf. notes to
26918 selection lxxii., p. 97.</p>
26919 <p>{@18-19. ut-sísṛpsatas (√sṛp)@} and {@ā-rúruk-@}
26920 {@ṣatas (√ruh),@} acc. pl. m. of ppl., 1027, 1029.
26921 {@--dyā́m,@} 361d. {@--dásyūṃr,@} u. f. {@dásyūn,@}
26922 prop. {@dásyūṃs,@} see 209 and b and 338^2^. --
26923 See {@√dhū + ava@} and 706. --Cf. the legend
26924 at Odyssey xi. 305-20, and see Eggeling,
26925 SBE. xii. 286.</p>

26928 predicate of {@ví-anāśayas (√1 naś).@} --See
26929 {@úttara 1.@} ‘Becoming victorious (after {%or%} as

26969 <p>SELECTION XLVIII. RV. viii. 91. To
26970 Agni.--Stanzas 7, 8, 9 and 13, 14, 15 form

26984 mann, for {@dīti,@} q. v.; instances of the con-

26986 wig, ‘durch die künstlichen mittel des {@ghṛta,@}’
26987 see {@2dhītí.@} 3. Sāyaṇa takes it from {@√dhā,@}
26988 ‘put,’ and renders by {@nidhānais,@} ‘with the
26989 deposits’ of ghee. 4. Best taken as
26990 ‘draughts,’ {@√dhā,@} ‘drink,’ see {@1dhītí@} and
26991 reference, and cf. RV. x. 115. 1.</p>

27036 <p>SELECTION XLIX. RV. x. 9. 1-3. To the
27037 Waters.--Rubricated at 105^21^, funeral ser-
27038 vice. This hymn in the RV. has nine stan-
27039 zas; but the first three form a strophe which
27040 recurs very often in other texts: so TS.,
27041 thrice; MS. and VS., twice; K., SV., AV.,
27042 and TA.</p>
27043 <p>--The Gṛhya-sūtras prescribe the use of
27044 this strophe, with the pouring or sprinkling
27045 of water, at the wedding-ceremony, ŚGS.
27046 i. 14, in the choosing of a house-site, AGS.
27047 ii. 8. 12, in the consecration of a new dwelling,
27048 ib. ii. 9. 8, and in certain funeral rites, ib. iv. 6.
27049 14 = 105^21^. Indeed, so frequent is their use
27050 that they have a name and are called the
27051 “three {@āpohiṣṭhīya@} stanzas”--cf. 1215a.
27052 The modern Hindus use them daily in their
[Page377-a+ 49]
27053 {@mārjana,@} see Monier-Williams, as cited at
27054 p. 366.</p>
27055 <p>{@1-2. ṣṭhā,@} u. f. {@stha,@} 188a, 248c. {@--tā́s,@}
27056 see 82^9^N. {@--√1dhā@} 2 and 669. {@--cákṣase,@}
27057 {@√cakṣ,@} 970c. --Lit. ‘Bring us to vigor, to
27058 great gladness, to behold:’ attraction,
27059 982a.</p>
27060 <p>{@3-4.@} See {@√bhaj,@} caus. {@--uśatī́s,@} fem.
27061 ppl., {@√vaś.@}</p>
27062 <p>{@5-6.@} U. f. {@tásmai,@} the master of the new
27063 dwelling. {@--gamāma,@} 848^3^. {@--janáyathā@}
27064 (248c), ‘produce, i. e. bring:’ for aught the
27065 accent shows, it may be co-ordinate with
27066 {@jínvatha@} (595) or also not (see 594a).</p>
27067 <p>--‘For him may we satisfy you, to whose
27068 dwelling ye hasten, Waters, and bring us’--

27073 <p>SELECTION L. RV. x. 14. 1-2, and 7-12.
27074 Funeral-hymn.--Rubricated at 102^1, 21^, 103^12^
27075 --see p. 402. See Whitney's essay, On the
27076 Vedic doctrine of a future life, OLSt. i. 46-63
27077 (= {%Bibliotheca Sacra,%} 1859, xvi. 404f); also
27078 Zimmer, p. 408-22. On immortality as an
27079 Indo-European belief, see Kaegi, N. 265 and
27080 literature there cited, and N. 283a. On the
27081 funeral rites, see introduction to selection
27082 lxxv. and literature there cited. Translations
27083 of this hymn: Muir, v. 292; GKR. p. 146;
27084 and Whitney, l. c., p. 58.</p>
27085 <p>--Although {@maṇḍala@} x. on the whole is
27086 late, it yet contains antique passages; and
27087 among these the following seems to belong:
27088 so Roth. The hymn contains 16 stanzas, of
27089 which 13-16 are palpably later additions.
27090 Only 7-12 are actually prescribed by Āśva-
27091 lāyana for use in the funeral service; but 1
27092 and 2, although not rubricated, are given
27093 for their intrinsic interest.</p>
27094 <p>--The passage as it stands consists of an
27095 introductory summons to the assembled
27096 mourners (1-2); and of an address to the
27097 departed (7-8); then, after bidding the
27098 mourners disperse (stanza 9), the spokesman
27099 implores the favor of the kindly-disposed
27100 hounds of Yama, for the departed (11) and
27101 for the company (12).</p>
[Page377-b+ 49]
27102 <p>--In stanza 10, on the other hand, these
27103 hounds are conceived as ill-disposed crea-
27104 tures standing guard to keep the departed
27105 out of bliss--see p. 370; and possibly the
27106 stanza which originally belonged with 9 has
27107 been displaced by the one in our text. Or
27108 is stanza 9 itself the intruder ? see Bezzen-
27109 berger's {%Beiträge,%} viii. 202.</p>
27110 <p>--The stanzas of RV. x. 14 recur else-
27111 where, with more or less interesting variants.
27112 Those in the Reader correspond respectively
27113 with AV. xviii. 1. 49, 50, 54; 3. 58; 1. 55; 2. 11,
27114 12, 13. Compare also MS. iv. 14. 16; ii. 7. 11;
27115 TA. vi. 1. 1; 4. 2; 6. 1; 3. 1, 2. For st. 9, see
27116 note thereto, 83^15^.</p>
27117 <p>{@7-8. parā_īyivā́ṃsam, √i,@} 803, 783b^2^. --
27118 ‘Along after ({@ánu@}) i. e. unto the mighty
27119 heights,’ i. e. to the other world. {@--anu-@}
27120 {@paspaśānám, √1paś,@} 807. {@--vaivasvatám,@}
27121 cf. 85^13^N. So also in the Avesta, Yima is
27122 V's son. --‘Gatherer of the peoples;’ pre-
27123 cisely so in the Avesta, Vd. ii. 21, Yima makes
27124 a gathering ({@hañjamanem@}) of mortals. Cf.
27125 <gr></gr>, Preller, {%Gr. Myth.%}^3^ i. 660,
27126 Kaegi, N. 276. --Note that Yama is a king,
27127 and not, like Varuṇa (line 12), a god.</p>
27128 <p>--Yama is the first mortal (see {@yamá@} in
27129 vocab.); the first to reach the other world
27130 (AV. xviii. 3. 13); the leader of the endless
27131 train of them that follow him; and so the
27132 king of the blessed (see {@yamá-rājan@}). The
27133 comparison of Yama with the Avestan Yima
27134 is very interesting: see Roth, {%die Sage von%}
27135 {%Dschemschid,%} ZDMG. iv. 417-431; and SBE.
27136 iv. p. lxxv., and p. 10-21.</p>
27137 <p>--We read at AV. xviii. 4. 7,
27138 {@tīrthaís taranti praváto mahī́r íti,@}
27139 By passes pass they to the mighty beights, 'tis said.
27140 This is most interesting as a reminiscence of
27141 the same traditional material of which RV.
27142 x. 14. 1 is another outgrowth.</p>
27143 <p>{@9-10.@} ‘Yamus nobis perfugium primus
27144 repperit:’ {@gātúm,@} q. v., pregnantly, like <gr></gr>
27145 at John xiv. 2. {@--eṣā́ gávyūtis,@} i. e. the {@gātú,@}
27146 just mentioned. {@--ápa-bhartavā́ u:@} the
27147 {@pada-pāṭha@} reads {@-tavaí u@} (133); form of
27148 inf., 972^2^; use of inf., 982c. --For the
27149 thought, see an Avestan parallel, Kaegi, N.
27150 270. {@--yátrā,@} 248a. {@--parā_īyús,@} 783b^2^.
[Page378-a+ 49]
27151 {@--jajñānā́s, √jaṇ,@} 807, ‘(their) children,’ sc.
27152 {@párā yanti.@}</p>

27172 <p>{@15-16.@} U. f. {@ápa_ita, ví_ita, ví ca sar-@}

27177 <p>--Pāda {%c.%} ‘(A place of rest) adorned
27178 with days, with waters, with nights:’ i. e.
27179 where the delights of earth are found again,
27180 the change of day and night, cooling waters,
27181 etc. Note the especial mention of waters in
27182 the enumeration of the joys of heaven at
27183 RV. ix. 113. 7f, and in the description of the
27184 ‘Assembly-hall’ of Yama Vaivasvata, MBh.
27185 ii. 8. 7 = 317. And observe how very frequent
27186 are the allusions to the waters in the men-
27187 tions of Paradise in the Koran, e. g. xlvii.
27188 10-20; lxxvi. 5; xiii. 35.</p>
27189 <p>--This pāda at best is bad. It is avoided
27190 by the noteworthy, but not very helpful,
27191 variants of the Yajurveda. VS. xii. 45 reads
27192 {@ápeta vī̀ta ví ca sarpatā́to@}
27193 {@yé átra sthá purāṇā́ yé ca nū́tanāḥ.@}
27194 {@ádād yamó avasā́nam pṛthivyā́@}
27195 {@ákrann imám pitáro lokám asmai,@}
27196 and substantially so read MS., TS., TB. The
27197 scholiast to VS. takes this as an address to
27198 ‘Yama's men.’ It {%is%} a little forced to refer
27199 it to the mourners.</p>
[Page378-b+ 48]
27200 <p>{@17-18.@} In {%this%} stanza, these dogs, the off-
27201 spring of Saramā, are ill-disposed (dur-
27202 vidátra)--see introduction. --Pronounce
27203 {@śuā́nau.@} --‘Four-eyed:’ later this is taken
27204 to mean ‘with two eyes and two round spots
27205 above the eyes.’ Note the Parsi custom of
27206 having such a dog (cf. Vd. viii. 16) view the
27207 corpse before exposure--Monier-Williams,
27208 {%Mod. India,%} 173-6, ed. 1878.</p>
27209 <p>--Respecting Saramā, see Kaegi, N. 149.
27210 Respecting the dogs, see ib., N. 274, 274a,
27211 and Muir, v. 294. Homer mentions the ‘dog
27212 of Hades,’ Od. xi. 623f, Il. viii. 368. Not till
27213 later is he called ‘many-headed’ and <gr></gr>.
27214 With <gr></gr> is identified {@śabála@}--see Ben-
27215 fey, {%Göttingische Nachrichten,%} 1877, p. 8f =
27216 {%Vedica,%} i. 149f. In Avestan belief (Vd.
27217 xiii. 9), dogs guard the entrance of the other
27218 world. These beliefs are compared, SBE.
27219 iv. p. lxxxvii.</p>
27220 <p>{@19.@} In this stanza and the following, the
27221 dogs are kindly creatures.</p>

27223 <p>{@1.@} Pronounce {@tā́bhiām.@} --Verbs, 668.</p>
27224 <p>{@2-3.@} U. f. {@asutṛ́pau:@} admits several ex-
27225 planations--see vocab., and Bergaigne, iii.
27226 72. --These messengers go about to con-
27227 duct to the other world those who have
27228 received the summons of Yama. {@--sū́ryāya,@}
27229 attraction, explained at 982a. --U. f. {@púnar@}

27233 <p>SELECTION LI. RV. x. 16. Funeral-hymn,
27234 used at the ceremony of cremation.--Rubri-
27235 cated are only stanzas 1-9 and 14--see p.
27236 402. The rubricated stanzas are translated
27237 by Max Müller, ZDMG. ix. p. viiif, and the
27238 whole hymn by Zimmer, p. 402. Most of
27239 the stanzas are addressed to Agni; but 3, 6,
27240 and 7, to the dead man. The hymn has two
27241 parts, clearly sundered by the metre.</p>
27242 <p>--In the first, Agni is to burn the corpse,
27243 not rudely, but gently, just enough to “make
27244 it done.” To this end, a sacrificial goat
27245 (st. 4) is provided, and (st. 7) the corpse is
27246 covered with a caul, in order that Agni may
27247 spend his fury on these things and spare the
[Page379-a+ 50]
27248 corpse from too severe a burning. The
27249 original purpose of this custom may have
27250 been different. The Greeks had the custom
27251 --see Il. xxiii. 165f, Od. xxiv. 65f.</p>
27252 <p>Moreover, Agni is to carry up the departed
27253 to the Fathers or Manes, who have their seat
27254 in the ‘fore-heaven’ (AV. xviii. 2. 48, Whit-
27255 ney, OLSt. i. 59). Perhaps st. 8-10 did not
27256 belong originally to the first part.</p>
27257 <p>--The second part consists of heterogene-
27258 ous material, vagrant stanzas, having some
27259 connection, verbal or logical, real or apparent,
27260 with the words or ideas of the first.</p>
27261 <p>--Every stanza of the hymn, save st. 11,
27262 appears in the AV., and some elsewhere also,
27263 and with interesting variants.</p>
27264 <p>{@4-5. enam:@} the minds of all present are
27265 so exclusively upon the departed, that the
27266 first reference to him may be made by
27267 an accentless and gestureless word without
27268 unclearness. Similarly {@asya. --ví dahas,@}
27269 {@abhí śocas,@} 743, 579. The AV. reads {@śūśucas@}
27270 (869). {@--cikṣipas,@} 869, 579. {@--kṛṇávas,@}
27271 700. --U. f. {@átha īm enam. --prá hiṇutāt:@}
27272 {@ṇ,@} 192c; form, 570, 704, cf. 91^5^; mg, 571.</p>
27273 <p>{@6-7. Pāda@} {%d%} = {@atha devān vaśaṃ neṣya-@}
27274 {@ti,@} ‘he shall bring the gods into his control’
27275 (cf. RV. x. 84. 3), i. e. ‘win their favor.’</p>
27276 <p>{@8-9.@} Note the combination of {@triṣṭubh@}
27277 and {@jagatī pādas@} (11, 12, 12, 11). Pronounce
27278 {@diā́m.@} --See {@dhárman. --apás, óṣadhīṣu,@}
27279 cf. x. 58. 7. --See {@hitá@} 2. {@--śarīrais,@} Sāyaṇa,
27280 {@‘śarīra_avayavais’@} (‘members’).</p>
27281 <p>--For this stanza there is abundant and
27282 interesting illustrative material. See Muir,
27283 v. 298, 319; Kaegi, N. 275, 275a. Man is a
27284 microcosm. Each element in him comes
27285 from some element in nature with which it
27286 has most affinity and thereto it returns (cf.
27287 Eurip. Suppl. 532f). These affinities are
27288 pointed out with much detail in ŚB. xiv.
27289 6. 2^13^.</p>
27290 <p>{@yatra_asya puruṣasya mṛtasya_agniṃ@}
27291 {@vāg apyeti, vātam prāṇaś, cakṣur ādityam,@}
27292 {@manaś candraṃ, diśaḥ śrotram, pṛthivīṃ@}
27293 {@śarīram, ākāśam ātamā_, oṣadhīr lomāni,@}
27294 {@vanaspatīn keśā, apsu lohitaṃ ca retaś ca@}
27295 {@nidhīyate,--kva_ayaṃ tadā puruṣo bha-@}
27296 {@vati ?@} ‘In case the dead man's…soul goes
27297 to the ether, the hair of his body to the
[Page379-b+ 50]
27298 plants, the hair of his head to the trees, and
27299 his blood and seminal fluid in the waters are
27300 put, what then becomes of this spirit ?’</p>
27301 <p>Cf. the formula recited at the slaughter of
27302 the sacrificial victim, AB. ii. 6. 13,
27303 {@sūryaṃ cakṣur gamayatāt,@}
27304 {@vātam prāṇam anvavasṛjatāt,@}
27305 {@antarikṣam asum,@}
27306 {@diśaḥ śrotram,@}
27307 {@pṛthivīṃ śarīram.@}
27308 Dissolution into the five elements (see {@bhūta@})
27309 is later the stereotyped phrase for death (see
27310 {@pañcatva@}). Cf. the four elements <gr></gr>
27311 <gr></gr>
27312 <gr></gr>, Plato, {%Timaeus,%} p. 82.</p>
27313 <p>--The affinity of the eye and the sun is
27314 universally palpable: cf., for example, Plato,
27315 {%Repub.%} 508, <gr></gr>
27316 <gr></gr>. Not less so
27317 is that of breath and wind. Bones and earth,
27318 {%Timaeus,%} 73E; blood and plants, ib. 80E. Cf.
27319 Darmesteter, SBE. iv. 187, who cites Iliad
27320 vii. 99 and Empedocles, 378-82 (ed. Müllach).</p>
27321 <p>{@10-11.@} ‘The goat [laid limb by limb on
27322 the corpse on the fire] (is thy) portion.
27323 Burn it with burning; that let thy heat
27324 burn; that, thy flame.’--But deal gently
27325 with the dead man. --Note the emphatic
27326 position of the last two {@tám's@} --The goat
27327 is the animal most fit for sacrifice--see the
27328 legend, AB. ii. 8. Later {@ajá@} is taken as {@a-já,@}
27329 the ‘unborn’ part: so Sāyaṇa; cf. Ludwig,
27330 iii. p. 435-6; Pañcatantra, book iii., fable 2;
27331 MBh. xii. 338. 3 (= 12820) fol. 255^b^; ISt. i. 428.</p>
27332 <p>--Agni has ‘dreadful forms’ ({@ghorā́s@}
27333 {@tanúas@}) as well as ‘kindly’ ones. --See
27334 {@loká@} 2b and {@sukṛ́t.@} The Hindus regard {@u@}
27335 as a particle; but it may be part of a word
27336 {@uloká,@} which combination appears in old
27337 texts, and even at the head of a {@pāda,@} where
27338 u (as enclitic) could not stand.</p>
27339 <p>{@12-13.@} See {@√hu + ā.@} ‘Who, offered to
27340 thee, goes freely.’ The corpse may have
27341 been conceived--now as yielding easily to
27342 the devourer, and now as struggling against
27343 it. Otherwise Zimmer, p. 403N.</p>
27344 <p>--‘Putting on life (as a garment), let him
27345 seek after offspring.’ Where is to be found
27346 expressed the wish for children in the new
27347 life ? It is repulsive. But that carnal inter-
[Page380-a+ 50]
27348 course was by no means excluded from the
27349 (doubtless later) ideas of heaven would
27350 appear from the material gathered by
27351 Muir, v. 307-9, esp. from AV. iv. 34. 2 and
27352 xiv. 2. 32. Add AB. i. 22. 14, and cf. Zimmer,
27353 413.</p>
27354 <p>--After all, may not {@śéṣas@} here mean
27355 simply ‘those whom we leave behind us,’
27356 viz. at the grave (see 63^17, 18^), in order to
27357 continue the journey of life without them,
27358 i. e. ‘those who have gone before ?’ These
27359 the dead man is now to rejoin.</p>
27360 <p>{@--Pāda@} {%d:%} cf. 83^14^ and N.</p>
27361 <p>{@14-15. kṛṣṇáḥ śakunás@} is one of ill-
27362 omen. {@--utá vā:@} function of {@utá@} like that
27363 of {@átha@} in {@átha vā,@} see {@atha6.@} --U. f. {@agnís@}
27364 {@tád@} (80^15^N.) {@viśva_át.@} --See {@brāhmaṇá:@}

27372 <p>{@16-17.@} Rubricated at 102^20^. Cutting out

27375 with it, for the purpose mentioned in the

27381 {@vi-dhakṣyán, √dah,@} fut. ppl. --Subjunctive

27383 <p>--Lit., then, ‘Wind a protection ({@várma@})
27384 from the fire (abl.) around thyself by means
27385 of the caul etc., i. e. envelop thyself with the
27386 caul as a protection from the fire [addressed
27387 to the dead man, although the celebrant
27388 actually does the enveloping]; cover thyself

27447 <p>SELECTION LII. RV. x. 17. 1-2 and 3-6.
27448 Funeral-hymn.--Under x. 17 are included
27449 divers elements: {@A.@} the fragmentary legend
27450 of the Children of the Sun (1-2); {@B.@} the
27451 funeral-hymn proper (3-6); {@C.@} a prayer to
27452 Sarasvatī (7-9); {@D.@} sundry fragments.</p>
27453 <p>{@--A.@} STANZAS 1-2. This famous fragment
27454 begins a new {@anuvāka@} and has nothing to
27455 do with the funeral-hymns. It smacks of
27456 antiquity; and it has become the nucleus of
27457 later legends. Of these, the oldest is that
27458 reported by Yāska, in the Nirukta, xii. 10;
27459 and the next is that of Śaunaka, in the
27460 Bṛhaddevatā, vi. 33 to vii. 2. Ś's version is
27461 quoted in full by Sāyaṇa, in his comment to
27462 RV. vii. 72. 2, in order to prove that the Rishi
27463 Vasiṣṭha was a relative (first cousin) of the
27464 Aśvins. Both legends are given in the
27465 original and in translation by Muir, v. 227-9;
27466 also by Kuhn, KZ. i. 440-43; L. Myrian-
27467 theus, {%Die Aśvins oder arischen Dioskuren,%}
27468 Munich, 1876, p. 1-4; and in substance by
27469 Max Müller, {%Lectures,%} 2d series, no. xi., p.
27470 501 Am. ed. of 1865 = 528 Eng. ed. of 1873.
27471 Late form of legend, VP., b'k iii., chap. 2.</p>
27472 <p>--The verbal exegesis of the two stanzas
27473 is beset with uncertainties: see Roth, in the
27474 essay cited above (83^8^N.), ZDMG. iv. 425;
27475 Grassmann, Transl., ii. p. 466; Bergaigne, ii.
27476 318; and the very suggestive discussions by
27477 Ludwig, iii. 332-5 and v. 391-2.</p>
27478 <p>--As for their interpretation from the
27479 mythological point of view, see Roth, l. c.
27480 p. 425 (reported by Müller, l. c., p. 503 = 530);
27481 ISt. xiv. 392f; Kuhn, l. c., p. 443f; Müller, l. c.,
27482 p. 528 = 556, and 502 = 529; Grassmann, l. c.;
27483 Bergaigne, ii. 506-7; and esp. Ludwig, iii.
27484 332-5 and v. 391-2.</p>
27485 <p>{@12.@} U. f. {@íti_idám.@} --Note that {@pari-@}
27486 {@uhyámānā@} is from the same root as {@vah-@}
27487 {@atúm. --Tvaṣṭar,@} a god, gives the wedding;
27488 yet it takes place on the earth !</p>
27489 <p>{@13. yamásya mātā́:@} proleptically; prop.,
27490 she who afterwards became Y's mother.</p>
27491 <p>{@14. kṛtvī́,@} 993b. {@--adadus,@} 668.</p>
27492 <p>{@15.@} U. f. {@utá_aśvínau abharat yád tád@}
27493 {@ā́sīt | ájahāt (2hā 1) u. --dvā́ mith-,@} ‘two
27494 pairs’ (Yama, Yamī; Aśvins): Yāska, ‘the
27495 two (Y. and Y.) that formed a pair’ (‘she
27496 forsook’).
[Page381-b+ 52]
27497 “Tvaṣṭar's making a wedding for his daughter”--
27498 At this news all the world here comes together.
27499 Yama's mother, during her wedding,
27500 The wife of mighty Vivasvant, vanished.
27501 They hid away the immortal from mortals.
27502 Making a like one, they gave her to Vivasvant.
27503 And she bare the two Aśvins when that happened,
27504 And left two pairs behind her--Saraṇyū.</p>
27505 <p>--“A braw story, but unco short.” The
27506 actual text is tantalizingly fragmentary. We
27507 can hardly hope to recover the legend with
27508 any satisfactory completeness. Yāska gives
27509 it thus:</p>
27510 <p>Tvaṣṭar's daughter, Saraṇyū, bare twins
27511 (Yama and Yamī) to Vivasvant. She foisted
27512 upon him another female of the same appear-
27513 ance ({@sávarṇām@}), and, taking on the form
27514 of a mare, fied forth. Vivasvant took on
27515 the form of a horse, followed her, and
27516 coupled with her. From that were born the
27517 two Aśvins or ‘Horse-men.’ Of the {@sávarṇā@}
27518 was born Manu.</p>
27519 <p>That is--Vivasvant, the Sun, and Saraṇyā,
27520 were the parents of Yama and Yamī (83^8^,
27521 92^14^), the first human pair. But there was
27522 a coexisting belief in Manu (see {@manu@} in
27523 vocab., and cf. SBE. xxv. p. lvii) as the father
27524 of mankind. Are not RV. x. 17. 1-2 the frag-
27525 ment of a legend which attempted to recon-
27526 cile the two beliefs by fabling a {@sávarṇā@}
27527 who should give birth to Manu, so that,
27528 according to either myth, the human race
27529 are the Children of the Sun ?</p>
27530 <p>--Yāska {%tells%} more than does the text; yet
27531 we are not sure that he (to say nothing of
27532 Śaunaka) {%knew%} anything more than is con-
27533 tained in the two stanzas. We are therefore
27534 not obliged to interpret the stanzas so as to
27535 fadge with Yāska's story.</p>
27536 <p>A possible rendering of line 14 is (see
27537 Ludwig): ‘They disclosed (<gr></gr>) the
27538 immortal to mortals ({%dat.%}). Endowing her
27539 with visible form, they gave her to V.’
27540 Lit. ‘making her (to be) {@sá-varṇa,@}’ i. e. (see
27541 {@2sa@} and 1304c) ‘making her (to be) having
27542 an accompanying {@varṇa@} {%or%} making her (to
27543 be) endowed with external appearance.’</p>
27544 <p>{@B.@} STANZAS 3-6. The funeral-hymn
27545 proper, addressed esp. to Pūṣan <gr></gr>,
27546 and rubricated at 103^13^. Respecting Pūṣan,
27547 see Kaegi, p. 55(77), and notes 209-12. As
27548 sun-god and heavenly herdsman, he knoweth
[Page382-a+ 48]
27549 well the ways through the heaven and the
27550 spaces, and so is a safe guide to conduct
27551 the souls of the dead to the regions of the
27552 blessed. Translation by Muir, v. 173.</p>
27553 <p>{@16-17.@} U. f. {@tvā@} (= the dead man) {@itás@}
27554 {@cyāvayatu prá@} (goes with {@cyāv-,@} 1081^2^)
27555 {@vidvā́n. --pári dadat,@} 650^3^.</p>
27556 <p>{@18-19. pári pāsati, √2pā,@} 893^2^. --See
27557 {@prápatha.@} --U. f. {@yátra ā́sate@} (628). --
27558 See {@sukṛ́t.@}</p>
27559 <p>{@20.@} U. f. {@imā́s ā́śās.@} --See {@√1vid + anu.@}
27560 {@--sárvās,@} see 77^11^N. {@--neṣat, √nī,@} 893^3^</p>
27561 <head>NOTES TO PAGE {@86.@}</head>
27562 <p>{@2-3. ajaniṣṭa,@} 902, {@√jaṇ;@} as we say, ‘he
27563 was “born and bred” there, i. e. is at home.’
27564 --‘On the distant-way of ways…: on the
27565 distant-way of heaven; {%etc.%}’ No matter how
27566 long or where the journey is, P. is at home
27567 on it.</p>
27568 <p>--‘Unto the two most wonted places--
27569 both to and from, he goes, knowing the way.’
27570 The construction is faulty--and yet plain
27571 enough: {@abhí sadhásthe@} harmonizes with {@ā́@}
27572 {@carati;@} but {@párā carati@} requires {@sadhásthe-@}
27573 {@bhyas@} (abl.).</p>
27574 <p>SELECTION LIII. RV. x. 18. Funeral-
27575 hymn.--Rubricated, all except the last stan-
27576 za, in the later ritual--see p. 402. The
27577 simple ceremonies originally used are in-
27578 ferrible from the hymn. Very different are
27579 those of the later ritual. For illustrations
27580 of these differences, see Roth, ZDMG. viii.
27581 471f. The hymn is given, with the concord-
27582 ants and variants of the AV., TB., TA., and
27583 VS., and the scholia of Sāyaṇa and Mahī-
27584 dhara to the concordant passages, by Win-
27585 disch, in his {%Zwölf Hymnen%} (see p. xviii,
27586 no. 10 of my {%Brief List%}).</p>
27587 <p>--The hymn has been translated by Roth,
27588 ZDMG. viii. 467f (reprinted by Zimmer, 404f);
27589 Max Müller, ZDMG. ix., appendix; H. H.
27590 Wilson, JRAS. xvi. 201f = {%Works,%} ii. 270f;
27591 Whitney, {%Bibliotheca Sacra,%} xvi. 409 = OLSt.
27592 i. 46f; GKR. p. 150; Ludwig, no. 943; Grass-
27593 mann, no. 844; Rājendralāla Mitra, {%Indo-%}
27594 {%Aryans,%} ii. 122f; and most of it by Kaegi,
27595 76(105)f. Roth, Whitney, GKR., and Kaegi
27596 give “the action” of the hymn.</p>
[Page382-b+ 50]
27597 <p>--The hymn is remarkable for its intrinsic
27598 interest and beauty. And it has acquired
27599 great notoriety in connection with the dis-
27600 cussions of Suttee ({@= satī@}) or Hindu widow-
27601 burning. Properly, {@satī@} (see {@sant 4@}) means
27602 a ‘virtuous wife.’ Improperly, but more
27603 commonly, it has come to be used of the
27604 rite of self-immolation which she practised.
27605 See the admirable article {%Suttee%} in Col. H.
27606 Yule's {%Anglo-Indian Glossary,%} with some forty
27607 pertinent extracts.</p>
27608 <p>--That Suttee is an ancient custom appears
27609 from many references to it in classical au-
27610 thors. See Cicero, {%Tusc. disp.%} v. 27. 78; Pro-
27611 pertius, iv. 12. 15; Plutarch, {%Moralia,%} p. 499;
27612 Nicholas of Damascus, fr. 143 = frag. hist.
27613 graec., ed. Müller, iii. 463; Strabo, xv. 30, p.
27614 699; 62, p. 714; and esp. the story in Diodo-
27615 rus Siculus, xix. 33-34, according to which
27616 the rite is authenticated for 316 B. C. These
27617 passages are given in full by J. Grimm, in
27618 his masterly essay, {%Ueber das Verbrennen der%}
27619 {%Leichen, Abh. der Berliner Akad.,%} 1849, p. 261f
27620 {%= Kleinere Schriften,%} ii. 298f. And Peter von
27621 Bohlen, in {%Das alte Indien%} (1830), i. 293-302,
27622 cites a great deal of interesting pertinent
27623 literature.</p>
27624 <p>--The custom was abolished by the British
27625 during the administration of Lord William
27626 Bentinck, in 1829. The story of the aboli-
27627 tion is well told by H. H. Wilson, in his con-
27628 tinuation of James Mill's {%History of British%}
27629 {%India,%} vol. iii. (= ix.), 185-192. For descrip-
27630 tions, statistics, etc., see Parliamentary Doc's
27631 1821. xviii.; 1823. xvii.; 1824. xxiii.; 1825. xxiv.;
27632 and esp. the {%Calcutta Review,%} 1867, vol. xlvi.
27633 p. 221-261. Other descriptions in {%Quarterly%}
27634 {%Review,%} lxxxix. 257f; Shib Chunder Bose, {%The%}
27635 {%Hindoos as they are,%} chap. xxi.; {%Das Ausland,%}
27636 for 1857, p. 1057f.</p>
27637 <p>--From Müller's {%Essay on Comparative%}
27638 {%Mythology, Chips,%} ii. 34f, or {%Selected Essays,%}
27639 vol. i. (ed. of 1881), p. 333f, it would appear
27640 that the seventh stanza of our hymn had
27641 played a great rôle in Hindu history. At
27642 any rate, this idea is current, and seems
27643 traceable to the {%Essay.%} Here it is stated
27644 that the stanza was purposely falsified by an
27645 unscrupulous priesthood, and that a garbled
27646 version of it, reading {@agnéḥ@} for {@ágre,@} was
[Page383-a+ 50]
27647 directly responsible for the sacrifice of thou-
27648 sands of innocent lives. That the author is
27649 in error on these points is argued with great
27650 detail by Fitzedward Hall, JRAS. NS. iii. 183-
27651 192. He shows that the misreading can be
27652 traced to Raghunandana, ca. 1500 A. D., and
27653 no further; and that Suttee was deemed to be
27654 amply justified by warrants other than those
27655 of the Vedic {@saṃhitā,@} which was by no
27656 means the ultimate appeal for the mediæval
27657 Hindu.</p>
27658 <p>--In the literary discussions of Suttee, on
27659 the other hand, the stanza has indeed played
27660 a rôle. There is probably no other stanza
27661 in the Veda about which so much has been
27662 written. It was first cited, in mangled form
27663 and as sanction for Suttee, by Colebrooke,
27664 in 1794, {%On the duties of a faithful Hindu%}
27665 {%widow, Asiatick Researches,%} 1795, iv. 209-219
27666 {%= Essays,%} i. 133-140. It was discussed by
27667 Wilson, in 1854, in his paper {%On the supposed%}
27668 {%Vaidik authority%} for Suttee, JRAS. xvi. 201-
27669 14 {%= Works,%} ii. 270-92. In answer to this,
27670 Rājā Rādhākānta Deva, in 1858, endeavored
27671 to adduce good Vedic authority for the rite,
27672 JRAS. xvii. 209-17 (reprinted in Wilson's
27673 {%Works,%} ii. 293-305). The most exhaustive
27674 treatment of the various readings of the
27675 stanza is that by Hall, l. c. Finally must
27676 be mentioned the paper read by Rājendralāla
27677 Mitra in 1870, {%On the funeral ceremonies%} etc.,
27678 JASB. xxxix. 1. 241-264 (reprinted in his In-
27679 troduction to the TA., p. 33-58, and with
27680 additions in his {%Indo-Aryans,%} ii. 114-155); see
27681 esp. p. 257f (= 50f = 147f).</p>
27682 <p>--The Rigveda gives no warrant for the
27683 custom. Śaunaka, in the Bṛhad-devatā,
27684 furnishes important positive evidence against
27685 it (see {%Chips,%} ii. 37); and likewise Manu, v.
27686 156-8 (= 64^10^f, see N.). Cf. Kaegi, N. 51.</p>
27687 <p>--The hymn was originally used at a
27688 burial which was not preceded by cremation.
27689 The situation and action are as follows. The
27690 corpse lies on a raised place; and by it is
27691 the widow.</p>
27692 <p>STANZAS 1-2. The spokesman adjures
27693 Death to remove, and to harm not the
27694 living (1); and pronounces for them absolu-
27695 tion from impurity (2).</p>
27696 <p>STANZAS 3-4. The conductor of the cere-
[Page383-b+ 50]
27697 mony dwells with joy on the fact that,
27698 thanks to the efficacy of their prayers, they
27699 have not joined the company of the dead (3).
27700 Now, for the better safety of the survivors,
27701 and wishing them long life, he sets a stone
27702 near the grave as a symbolic boundary of
27703 the domain of Death, as a barrier, so that
27704 he may not pass to the space beyond or
27705 domain of the living.</p>
27706 <p>STANZAS 5-6. The wish and prayer for
27707 long life is here continued.</p>
27708 <p>STANZAS 7-8. The women are now sum-
27709 moned to make their appearance together,
27710 and, provided with ointments, ‘to go up to
27711 the place,’ i. e. of course, where the dead
27712 man and the widow are (7).</p>
27713 <p>Here we must infer that they adorn the
27714 widow (as a sign that she is to re-enter the
27715 world of life), and that the dead man's
27716 brother ({@devṛ́,@} ‘levir’) then takes her hand
27717 in token of the levirate marriage.</p>
27718 <p>The priest then bids her leave her lifeless
27719 spouse, and makes solemn declaration of the
27720 new relation into which she has entered (8).</p>
27721 <p>STANZA 9. The bow is now taken from
27722 the dead man's hand, in order that the power
27723 and glory of which the weapon was the
27724 symbol may remain with the survivors; and
27725 a closing benediction is said for them and
27726 for the departed.</p>
27727 <p>STANZAS 10-13. “And now, with gentle
27728 action and tender words, the body is com-
27729 mitted to the earth.”</p>
27730 <p>{@4-5. √i + anu-parā. --te suás,@} cf. 55^18^N.
27731 {@--ítara,@} w. abl., like {@anya,@} 3^4^. {@--devayā́nāt,@}
27732 ‘going {%or%} leading to the gods,’ sc. {@pathás,@}
27733 abl. --U. f. {@mā́…rīriṣas@} (80^10^N.), {@mā́ utá.@}
27734 Note how {@utá@} follows the repeated portion
27735 of the second clause.</p>
27736 <p>{@6-7.@} See {@padá@} 3. --Discussions of {@√yup,@}
27737 Ludwig, v. 514, Whitney, AJP. iii. 402, Roth,
27738 {%Festgruss an Böhtlingk,%} 1888, p. 98-99. {@--@}
27739 {@aíta,@} 620. --See {@√1dhā@} 7. {@--√pyā + ā.@}</p>
27740 <p>{@6^a^.@} Either ‘Clogging Death's foot [by a
27741 bundle of brush ({@kūdī́@}) or a billet of wood,
27742 tied to the corpse's foot], as ye came;’ or
27743 else, ‘Effacing Death's foot-print’ [by the
27744 same means]. I confess, I incline to the
27745 former view. But, whichever way we take
27746 {@padáṃ yop-,@} the simple symbolism amounts
[Page384-a+ 50]
27747 to the same thing. The clog is attached to
27748 the foot of the corpse, which represents
27749 Death, in order that Death may not get
27750 back or find his way back so easily to harm
27751 the living. See AV. v. 19. 12 (explained by
27752 Roth, l. c.) in connection with xii. 2. 29. Roth
27753 adduces the device of Hermes, in the Homeric
27754 Hymn to Hermes, 80-84.</p>
27755 <p>{@8-9.@} U. f. {@ví mṛtaís@} (283^2^) {@ā_avavṛtran@}
27756 {@(√vṛt + vy-ā):@} Whitney takes the form as a
27757 3d pl. (550^4^) impf. mid. of the redup. class,
27758 present-stem {@vavṛt,@} irreg. like {@cakṛ@} (expect
27759 {@vivṛt@}). {@--ábhūt,@} ‘hath been,’ 928. --Pāda {%c%}
27760 refers--not to “mirth in funeral,” but rather
27761 --to a fresh start on a new stretch of life,
27762 in which, leaving the dead behind, they look
27763 for much joy.</p>
27764 <p>{@10-11.@} Note radical connection of {@pari-@}
27765 {@dhím@} and {@dadhāmi.@} --U. f. {@mā́_eṣām nú@}
27766 {@gāt áparas@} (‘an other’). --‘A hundred
27767 autumns, numerous, may they live.’ Prayers
27768 like this are frequent. The love of long life
27769 is very clear in the Vedic texts as contrasted
27770 with those of the later period. We must not
27771 think of ‘hundred’ as just ‘99 + 1.’ --See
27772 {@√1dhā + antar,@} and note carefully the develop-
27773 ment of the mg. ‘May they hide Death by
27774 a mountain, i. e. put a mountain (symbolized
27775 by the rock or {@paridhí@}) betwixt themselves
27776 and Death.’ See {@párvata@} 2 and 4.</p>
27777 <p>--There is much evidence that the age of
27778 a hundred years was deemed entirely normal.
27779 This appears from RV. i. 89. 9. Again, to a
27780 question about a funeral lustration, Pāras-
27781 kara (PGS. iii. 10. 14, 15) prescribes the use of
27782 a special answer “in case the departed was
27783 not yet a hundred years old.” Weber, ISt.
27784 xvii. 500. SBE. xxix. 356. The ‘tenth decade
27785 of life’ has a name, {@daśamī@} (see BR.). In the
27786 Jātaka (Fausböll, vol. ii. p. 16), the Bodhisat
27787 says to his father, when the latter sneezes,
27788 O Gagga, live a hundred years,
27789 And twenty others added on.…
27790 Live thou a hundred autumns yet.
27791 See also Bhartṛhari's fine stanza, {@āyur-@}
27792 {@varṣaśataṃ nṛṇām@} etc., {@Vairāgya-śataka,@}
27793 50 Bohlen = 107 Telang.--Among Semitic
27794 peoples, the sacred age was 120 years; while
27795 the pious Egyptian prayed to Osiris that he
27796 might live to be 110.--Krall.</p>
[Page384-b+ 50]
27797 <p>--Very interesting are the classical stories
27798 of Hindu longevity. Sometimes, according
27799 to Onesikritos, they capped a hundred with
27800 thirty more--Strabo, xv. 34, p. 701. The Uttara
27801 Kurus are said, MBh. vi. 7. 11 = 264, to live
27802 1000 or 10,000 years, and to this fable is prob-
27803 ably due the report of Megasthenes <gr></gr>
27804 <gr></gr> in Strabo, xv. 57, p. 711
27805 --see McCrindle's Megasthenes, p. 79N., or
27806 Lassen, IA. i^2^. 613.</p>
27807 <p>--Note that years are counted, now by
27808 winters, now by autumns, and now by rainy
27809 seasons (see {@hima@} and {@varṣa@} in vocab.), and
27810 that these differences correspond in general
27811 with differences of habitat of the people.
27812 ISt. xvii. 232, Zimmer, 371-2.</p>
27813 <p>{@12-13.@} U. f. {@yáthā áhāni, yáthā ṛtávas@}
27814 (see 127^2^): pronounce {@yáthā́hāni…| yáth-@}
27815 {@artáva.@} --‘As a following one deserts not
27816 the former one, i. e. as each season lacks not
27817 a successor.’ --See {@evá@} 1: contrast 18^18, 19^.
27818 --See {@√kḷp:@} ‘so dispose their lives, i. e. make
27819 them move on in the same unbroken way.’</p>
27820 <p>{@14-15. yáti ṣṭhá@} (188a), ‘quot estis.’
27821 {@--karati,@} true root-aor. subjunctive, 836^1^.
27822 {@--jīváse,@} 970c, 982. --‘Attain ye to (long-
27823 life, {@ā́yus,@} i. e.) fulness of days, old age your
27824 portion making, one after another, in turn
27825 ({@yátamānās@}) all of you’ ({@yáti ṣṭhá@}). Other-
27826 wise OLSt. i. 53.</p>
27827 <p>{@16-17.@} STANZA 7. See introduction, and
27828 Kaegi, N. 328. {@--nā́rīs,@} classical {@nā́ryas,@} 365.
27829 2. --See {@√viś + sam.@} --Pāda {%d:%} ‘Let the
27830 wives ascend to the (raised) place (on which
27831 the corpse lies), to begin with’ ({@ágre@}).--
27832 After which, they are to do their errand, see
27833 introduction to stanzas 7-8. It does not
27834 mean, ‘Let them go (away from the bier)
27835 up to a sacrificial altar first, i. e. before
27836 others;’ for this we should expect {@pra-@}
27837 {@thamā́s@} rather than {@ágre.@}</p>
27838 <p>{@18-19.@} U. f. {@úd īrṣva (√īr) nāri…. --@}
27839 {@gatā́sum etám úpa śeṣe,@} lit. ‘With this one
27840 whose life is gone liest thou.’ --U. f. {@ā́_ihi.@}
27841 --‘To take the hand’ is the essential pre-
27842 liminary of wedding--see 89^5^N. --U. f.
27843 {@didhiṣós táva idám.@} See {@2idám. --pátyus@}
27844 (343b) {@janitvám@} means ‘condition of being
27845 {@jáni@} of a {@páti;@}’ and the whole line 19 (see
27846 {@bhū + abhi-sam@}) means simply ‘Thou hast
[Page385-a+ 49]
27847 entered into the state of being wife of a
27848 spouse (who was) a hand-grasper (and is)
27849 thy suitor now.’</p>
27850 <p>--Hillebrandt, at ZDMG. xl. 708f, shows
27851 plainly that this stanza, RV. x. 18. 8, belonged
27852 originally to the ritual of the human sacri-
27853 fice. Weber describes the ceremony at
27854 ZDMG. xviii. 269f = {%Indische Streifen,%} i. 65f.
27855 The king's first queen was obliged to lie
27856 with the dead victim. The situation is evi-
27857 dent from the connection in which RV. x.
27858 18. 8 occurs at AV. xviii. 3. 1-4. She is bidden
27859 to rise with our stanza, {@úd īrṣva nāri@}--see
27860 Śāṅkhāyana Śrauta-sūtra, xvi. 13. 13.</p>
27861 <p>In this light, the logical connection of
27862 pādas {%a b%} with pādas {%c d%} becomes clear. She
27863 is to forsake the corpse and “come hither”
27864 to the king.
27865 Rise up, woman, to the world of the living.
27866 Fled is the soul of him with whom thou liest. Come hither.
27867 Quitting the embrace of hateful Death, the
27868 queen rises and approaches him who had
27869 already once taken her hand in wedlock and
27870 now stands waiting for her as a suitor once
27871 more. Upon thus resuming her proper rela-
27872 tion of wife again, she is greeted with the
27873 words:
27874 To him who grasped thy hand, thy suitor now,
27875 As wife to husband art thou become related.</p>
27876 <p>--As appears from AGS. iv. 2. 18 = 102^7^,
27877 this stanza was at an early date appropriated
27878 for the funeral-service, where--as an accom-
27879 paniment of the levirate marriage--it fits
27880 very well ({@didhiṣú@} means also ‘a second
27881 husband’). Regarding leviration, see Kaegi,
27882 N. 51. Its existence in Vedic times is proved
27883 by RV. x. 40. 2.--Compare also Deuteronomy
27884 xxv. 5-10.</p>
27885 <p>{@20.@} U. f. {@dhánus hástāt ā-dádānas@} (668)
27886 {@mṛtásya | asmé@} (dat., 492^2^) {@kṣatrā́ya@} etc.
27887 ‘(I, the spokesman,) taking from the dead
27888 man's hand the bow, for us for power…i. e.
27889 that ours may be the power, glory, might’--.
27890 Here the construction breaks off short, but
27891 without a jot of unclearness. --Note that
27892 the bow is left in his hand till the very last.
27893 This was their noblest and chiefest weapon:
27894 cf. RV. vi. 75; the stories of Arjuna's bow,
27895 Gāṇḍīva; and Strabo, xv. 66, p. 717.</p>

27928 <p>--Pāda {%b:%} ‘And laying down this clod
27929 may I not get harm.’ This seems to refer
27930 to the {%glebam in os inicere%} (a custom which
27931 still accompanies the “earth to earth, ashes
27932 to ashes, dust to dust” of Christian burial),
27933 and to betray the natural “uncanny feeling
27934 at having to do with a corpse.” Cf. Kaegi,
27935 N. 330.</p>
27936 <p>--Pāda {%c.%} The ‘pillar’ or ‘prop’ may be
27937 a rude beam or tree, laid over the corpse so
27938 as to keep the earth from caving in on it:
27939 cf. AV. xviii. 2. 25,
27940 “Let not the tree press hard on thee,
27941 Nor yet the earth, the great, divine.”
27942 Sometimes the tree was hollowed out as a
27943 coffin (AV. xviii. 3. 70): cf. the Germanic
27944 {%Todtenbaum%} of sacred oak--Weinhold, {%Alt-%}
27945 {%nordisches Leben,%} 497, 491.</p>
[Page386-a+ 50]
27946 <p>{@10-11.@} U. f. {@áhani | íṣvās…ā́ dadhus.@}
27947 ‘On a fitting day me, as the plume of an
27948 arrow, have they set.’ --The stanza seems
27949 to express the poet's satisfaction at having
27950 made a good hymn at the right time and
27951 place and with as good skill as a skilful
27952 horseman has. Whitney renders,
27953 They've set me in a fitting day,
27954 As one the plume sets on the shaft.
27955 I've caught and used the fitting word,
27956 As one a steed tames with the rein.</p>
27957 <p>--The stanza is fully discussed, JAOS. xi.
27958 p. cxci = PAOS. May, 1884. It is interesting
27959 as illustrating the varieties of cumulative
27960 evidence that may be brought to bear on
27961 the criticism of the Veda. Thus: 1. The
27962 stanza is at the end of the hymn and out of
27963 connection. 2. It is in a different kind of
27964 metre. 3. The metre is bad of its kind.
27965 4. The form {@íṣvās@} is bad Vedic--for {@íṣos;@}
27966 and 5. {@pratī́cīm@} is a late form for {@pratīcī́m.@}
27967 6. The stanza is ignored by Āśvalāyana;
27968 and 7. by Sāyaṇa.</p>
27969 <p>SELECTION LIV. RV. x. 33. 4-9. The
27970 aged priest to the young prince.--The hymn
27971 has nine stanzas. The first three have nothing
27972 to do with the rest. The rest (4-9) forms
27973 two {@tṛca's.@} This passage has more than
27974 common freshness, and also directness of
27975 connection with the life of Vedic time. The
27976 situation would seem to be somewhat as
27977 follows.</p>
27978 <p>--The old priest stood well with the gods,
27979 so that the efficacy of his intercession with
27980 them was of unusually good repute. Accord-
27981 ingly, the foes of king Kuruśravaṇa had
27982 once tried to win the Rishi over to their side
27983 and away from his master, Kuruśravaṇa;
27984 but in vain. He had remained faithful to
27985 the royal family in whose service he long
27986 had been.</p>
27987 <p>Now at last king Kuruśravaṇa has passed
27988 away, leaving Upamaśravas as his son and
27989 heir. And in presence of the young prince,
27990 the priest tells with pride and pleasure of
27991 the old times, and speaks with regret of the
27992 loss of his departed patron.</p>
27993 <p>--Ludwig, iii. 182, has called attention to
27994 the genealogical series of the RV. These
27995 cover oftenest, of course, only three genera-
[Page386-b+ 53]
27996 tions, since memory, unaided by records,
27997 does not easily go further back. But for
27998 preserving that amount of genealogical tra-
27999 dition there was frequent need (Weber, ISt.
28000 x. 78-88, esp. 82): thus, at the offering to
28001 the Manes, the priest has to address by name
28002 the father, grandfather, and great-grand-
28003 father of the sacrificer; see ŚB. ii. 4. 2^16^ or
28004 SBE. xii. 365 or OLSt. i. 60: similarly at
28005 the {@pravara;@} cf. ISt. ix. 322-3 or x. 78-9 or
28006 Müller, ASL. 386.</p>
28007 <p>In the present instance, however, we have
28008 a series of five at least. Trasadasyu is a
28009 prince of the Pūru tribe, and of the {%line%} of
28010 Purukutsa (he is not necessarily the latter's
28011 son--RV. vii. 19. 3), and is often mentioned
28012 for his generosity and for the special favors
28013 shown him by the gods. The series is
28014 Purukutsa
28015 …
28016 Trasadasyu
28017 …
28018 Mitrātithi
28019 |
28020 Kuruśravaṇa
28021 |
28022 Upamaśravas.
28023 Compare Bergaigne, JA. 8. vi. 373-4, and
28024 Kaegi, 80 (110), and N. 340. The Pūrus were
28025 one of several tribes that were ultimately
28026 fused together in the famous Kurus--Olden-
28027 berg, {%Buddha,%} 403 = 411.</p>
28028 <p>{@12-13. āvṛṇi:@} augment, 585^2^; impf. mid.
28029 (725), 1st sing. ‘I, the Rishi, preferred
28030 king K.’ (to his enemies); i. e. I chose to
28031 keep him as my master, in order to go out
28032 to battle with him, etc. A choice was in-
28033 evitable. It would appear that priests who
28034 sacrificed for many or for a village were
28035 despised (Yājñavalkya, i. 161, 163, Manu, iv.
28036 205). The purohitas marched out with their
28037 kings to battle (AGS. iii. 12), as did the
28038 <gr></gr>, e. g. to the battle of Plataea (Hdt.
28039 ix. 33, 37), and for similar motives--<gr></gr>
28040 <gr></gr>.</p>
28041 <p>{@14-15. tisrás,@} 482c. {@--stávai@} (626, 617),
28042 ‘I will praise,’ sc. {@tám,@} meaning Kuru-
28043 śravaṇa. {@--sah-,@} sc. {@yajñé.@} --K's horses
28044 ({%trīga%}) still come to fetch the priest in state
28045 to the sacrifice.</p>
28046 <p>{@16-17. yásya:@} K. is still meant. {@--@}
28047 {@ūcúṣe,@} 803, {@√vac.@} --‘Of whom, (namely)
28048 of U's father, the words (were) highly pleas-
[Page387-a+ 48]
28049 ing to (me) the intercessor, as a lovely home.’
28050 [? The text is awkward and unclear.] Time
28051 was, when king K., to offset the overtures
28052 of his enemies, had to make very persuasive
28053 offers to the priest. No false delicacy re-
28054 strains the latter now from alluding to these
28055 with satisfaction in the presence of his future
28056 patron. --The mention of K. as ‘father of
28057 U.’ is peculiar. Somewhat analogous is the
28058 Semitic fashion: cf. {%Abd-allah,%} ‘Gott-schalk.’</p>
28059 <p>{@18-19. ádhi@} goes w. {@ihi, √i,@} q. v. --U. f.
28060 {@nápāt…pitús te@} (80^15^N.). {@--pitús@} limits
28061 {@vanditā́@} as a possessive (not objective) geni-
28062 tive. The objective gen. would be {@devā́nām.@}
28063 {@--asmi:@} the present does not necessarily
28064 imply that K. is still alive.</p>
28065 <p>{@20. yád ī́śīya,@} cf. 80^11^N. “Had it de-
28066 pended on me, my maghavan K. should have
28067 lived. But--.” --But for {@utá,@} the {@vā@}
28068 would have to follow {@mártiānaām@}--cf.
28069 {@atha6.@}</p>

28092 The first half tells what happens at the

28209 <p>--STANZA 6 is really a prayer to Agni and
28210 his flames to help in the work of devotion.
28211 As the immortal messenger (see Muir, v. 201)
28212 between men and gods, he is to go from
28213 earth, traverse the atmosphere (see {@rájas@} in
28214 vocab.), and pursue his way to the gods

28217 the prayers and oblations that go up to the

28219 the AB. at iii. 38 calls them, on which the
28220 gods descend to man. Pāda {%c,%} continuing
28221 the metaphor of {%a,%} is addressed to Agni's
28222 flames; and {%d,%} to Agni.
28223 Stretching devotion's weft from gloom to light go on.
28224 Keep to the radiant pathways which our prayers have made.
28225 Without a blemish weave ye now the singers' work.
28226 Be Manu thou. Bring to our sight the heavenly race.</p>

28241 <p>--STANZA 8. The situation is perhaps
28242 this. A band of men, hotly pursued by their
28243 enemies, are in the middle of a stream, which
28244 they hope soon to have put betwixt them-
28245 selves and the foe. They call out to each
[Page389-a+ 59]
28246 other encouragingly the words of the stanza.
28247 The famous hymn RV. iii. 33 involves a situ-
28248 ation which is similar (Muir, i^2^. 338), and,
28249 indeed, familiar elsewhere (Hebrews xi. 29;
28250 Hdt. viii. 138).
28251 The stream is stony. Hold ye well together.
28252 Your footing keep. On ! make your crossing, comrades !
28253 There let us leave them in a mood unhappy,
28254 While we go out and on to happy conquests.</p>

28300 fore the bride's departure for her new home.

28310 to Prajāpati on behalf of himself and his

28312 good wishes and solemn benedictions (43 {%c d,%}

28314 fruitful (45); bids her hold her own with her
28315 new relatives (46); and, finally, beseeches
28316 the gods, on behalf of himself and his wife,
28317 that they two may dwell in unity (47).</p>
28318 <p>{@5-6. gṛbhṇā́mi…hástam:@} this, the dex-
28319 trarum iunctio, is the essential feature of
28320 the simplest wedding-ritual, see Haas, 277,
28321 316; cf. {@hasta-grābhá@} (86^19^) and {@pāṇi-grāha@}
28322 (64^10^); the AV. modernizes, reading, {@gṛhṇā́mi.@}
28323 With the concordant stanza of the AV. are
28324 grouped several others (48, 49, 51) of like
28325 import. --‘With me as husband’ (343b).
28326 {@--yáthā_ásas,@} ‘ut sis.’ --U. f. {@máhyam@}
28327 {@tvā_adus@} (829). {@--gā́rh-,@} ‘for (our) being
28328 heads of a household, i. e. that we may
28329 establish a family.’</p>
28330 <p>{@7-8. ábadhnāt,@} 730. --See {@ṛtá@} 2. --See
28331 {@loká@} 2b and cf. {@sukṛtá@} with {@sukṛ́t.@} This
28332 phrase is equiv. in {%form%} to the older one
28333 (84^11^), but refers here rather to the ‘world
28334 of the pious’ on earth.
28335 I loose thee from Varuṇa's bond,
28336 With which kindly Savitar bound thee.--
28337 At the altar, in the company of the good,
28338 I put thee unharmed with thy husband.</p>
28339 <p>--The first half-stanza is an allegorical,
28340 and the second a literal address to the bride.
28341 In the allegory, the bond of Varuṇa is night
28342 (Hillebrandt). Savitar is the ‘Impeller,’ not
28343 only of the rising, but also of the setting sun
28344 (RV. i. 35. 3^a^). He brings rest (ii. 38. 3, 4) by
28345 sending night, whose gentle bond he lays
28346 (Muir, v. 235-7) upon his daughter, Sūryā,
28347 ‘The Sun,’ till she is released for her bridal
28348 with Soma, ‘The Moon’ (masc.), a union
28349 which is the prototype (cf. Haas, 328) of
28350 human marriages.</p>
28351 <p>From the more literal point of view,
28352 the bond of {@Varuṇa,@} as the upholder of the
28353 established order of things, is (not night--
[Page390-a+ 49]
28354 still less sickness--but) the tie by which a
28355 maid is bound to her father till a man come
28356 to loose and take her.--See the discussions
28357 of Haas, 319-20, 277-8, and of Hillebrandt,
28358 {%Varuṇa,%} p. 59.</p>

28362 is quite natural) {@tvā́m.@} --U. f. {@amútas@} (171^4^)
28363 {@karam@} (831^2^) {@| yáthā_iyám…ásati,@} ‘ut

28367 {@gṛhāt pramuñcāmi. amuto bhartṛ-gṛhe@}

28436 <p>SELECTION LIX. RV. x. 137. Exorcism

28444 pares similar Vedic and Germanic spells.

28450 sponds in general to AV. iv. 13; but see note

28473 <p>--STANZA 6. In place of this stanza the
28474 AV. has interpolated RV. x. 60. 12, evidently
28475 because it has to do with the laying on of
28476 hands--see the following.</p>
28477 <p>{@2-3.@} That the laying on of hands has
28478 especial virtue is a wide-spread belief--cf.
28479 Acts viii. 17f, and above, p. 369^a^ top. The
28480 Greeks attributed to each of the <gr></gr>
28481 <gr></gr> a name and a particular healing power.
28482 The finger next to the little finger (see note
28483 to 104^11^) is called, {%digitus medicus%} by Pliny,
28484 and {%medicinalis,%} in the laws of Henry I. of
28485 England; and it has a special and beneficent
28486 magic power.--W. Grimm, {%Kleinere Schriften,%}
28487 iii. 440f, 442.</p>
28488 <p>--‘With hands, ten-fingered (the tongue
28489 is leader of the charm), healing,--thee with
28490 these thee we touch.’ The parenthesis may
28491 mean that the tongue brings out a charm to
28492 precede the laying on of hands. Text prob-
28493 ably corrupt; AV. variants interesting.</p>
28494 <p>SELECTION LX. RV. x. 154. To Yama.--
28495 Funeral-hymn.--Rubricated at 103^13^, see p.
28496 402. Recurs at AV. xviii. 2. 14-18, with inter-
28497 esting variants. Translated, Muir, v. 310.
28498 To judge from the ritual, the subject of {@ápi@}
28499 {@gacchatāt@} in each stanza is the spirit of the
[Page391-b+ 48]
28500 departed. He is to go and join the Fathers
28501 --saints, warriors, poet-sages,--a descrip-
28502 tion of whom makes up most of the hymn.
28503 In stanzas 4 and 5, {@gacchatāt@} (see 570) is a
28504 3d sing., ‘O Yama, let him go;’ in the rest,
28505 it may be a 2d or a 3d.--Ludwig, ii. 394,
28506 v. 311, interprets otherwise, taking {@mádhu@} as
28507 subject of {@ápi gacchatāt (√gam + api).@}</p>
28508 <p>{@4-5.@} ‘Some…others’ ({@éke@}), i. e. of the
28509 Fathers, “who revel in bliss with Yama”
28510 (83^18^). --See {@√ās + upa.@} --‘For whom {@mádhu@}
28511 flows:’ Yama and the Fathers are the eager
28512 recipients of sweet drink-offerings--cf. AV.
28513 xviii. 2. 1-3, and RV. x. 15 passim. {@--tā́ṃś@}
28514 cid = ‘thóse:’ {@tā́ṃś cid evá@} = ‘júst those.’</p>
28515 <p>{@6-7.@} Pronounce {@súar.@} --‘Who made
28516 {@tápas@} their glory.’ --Heaven can be won
28517 only by the pious and (stanza 3) the brave:

28519 <p>{@10-11.@} See {@ciḍ.@} --U. f. {@pū́rve ṛtasā́pas.@}
28520 --Cf. 85^4^.</p>
28521 <p>{@12-13.@} ‘Who keep (= confine themselves

28541 <p>SELECTION LXII. MS. ii. 13. 23. Hiraṇya-
28542 garbha.--The god Ka or Who (see notes to
28543 selection lxviii.).--The RV. version (x. 121)
28544 of this famous hymn has been translated by
28545 Max Müller, ASL. 569, and {%Chips,%} i. 29; by
28546 Ludwig, no. 948; and by Muir, with com-
28547 ments, iv^2^. 15-18.</p>
[Page392-a+ 50]
28548 <p>--STRUCTURE of the hymn. The seven
28549 stanzas here given (or perhaps only 2-6)
28550 constitute the original stock of the hymn.
28551 To this is added, in the MS.-version, an
28552 eighth stanza, quite impertinent to the rest;
28553 and in the RV.-version, three stanzas (8, 9,
28554 10), whose character is determinable by
28555 various and interesting criteria.</p>
28556 <p>Pāda {%d%} of each stanza is a refrain, ‘Whom
28557 as god shall we worship with oblation ? i. e.
28558 Who is the god that we are to worship with
28559 oblation ?’ The later Vedic texts under-
28560 stand it, ‘We will worship the god Who or
28561 Ka,’ making of the interrogative pronoun a
28562 deity whom they identify with Prajāpati
28563 (selection lxviii.). The other pādas of stan-
28564 zas 2-6 are relative clauses with {@yás, yásya,@}
28565 {@yéna, yásmin.@} These may refer</p>
28566 <p>{@A.@} To {@devā́ya:@} ‘Who (interrog.) is the
28567 god,--who (relative) became king, etc. etc.,
28568 --that we are to worship ?’ Ludwig: ‘Ka,
28569 the god,--who is become king, etc. etc.,--
28570 we would wait upon with havis.’--Better,
28571 perhaps,</p>
28572 <p>{@B.@} To {@hiraṇyagarbhás,@} although stanza
28573 1 (and 7 as well) looks as if it might have
28574 been an afterthought. This leaves the con-
28575 nection of the refrain with the rest of each
28576 stanza very loose; but this is perhaps just
28577 what it ought to be.</p>
28578 <p>--The hymn corresponds to RV. x. 121.
28579 The comparative study of the differeing ver-
28580 sions of the same traditional material, as it
28581 appears in different Vedic texts, is interest-
28582 ing and instructive; and there is no better
28583 opportunity for it than this (see Preface,
28584 p. v, N. 2).</p>
28585 <p>The hymn occurs also at TS. iv. 1. 8; in the
28586 VS., with the stanzas scattered, at xiii. 4,
28587 xxiii. 3, xxv. 13, xxv. 12, xxxii. 6, 7, xxvii. 25;
28588 and at AV. iv. 2. The TS. version follows
28589 that of the RV. most nearly. Disregarding
28590 the order, the same is true of the VS. The
28591 AV. version looks like an unsuccessful
28592 attempt at writing down a half-remembered
28593 piece.</p>

28595 designates the acute accent; the horizontal
28596 hook, the circumflex. Details in L. von
28597 Schroeder's ed., book i., preface, p. XXIX.
[Page392-b+ 48]
28598 Phonetic peculiarities, ibidem, XXVIII.

28600 marked by the ‘dot in the creseent,’ e. g.
28601 {@praśíṣam,@} line 4. See also Preface to this
28602 Reader, p. v, note 7.</p>

28663 <p>SELECTION LXIII. MS. i. 5. 12. Legend
28664 of Yama and Yamī.--The creation of night.
28665 --Respecting Yama and Yamī, see notes to
28666 85^12^f.</p>
28667 <p>--The prose of the Brāhmaṇas is not difficult. In
28668 reading it, the chief thing is some familiarity with
28669 the style, i. e. ability to divide up the discourse aright
28670 into the little clauses and choppy sentences with
28671 which it proceeds. As a help to this it is important
28672 to observe that the particle {%átha%} marks the beginning
28673 of a new clause, and that the postpositive {%vai%} marks
28674 the foregoing word as the first of its clause. Analo-
28675 gous is the use of {%nāma%} to distinguish a proper name
28676 from an identical appellative. Cf. Pliny, {%Epp.%} vi. 31,
28677 evocatus in consilium ad centum cellas (hoc loco
28678 nomen)….</p>
28679 <p>As my colleague, Professor A. P. Peabody, has
28680 observed in his translations of Cicero's {%Offices%} and
28681 {%Tusculans,%} there are certain connectives and illatives
28682 which are employed as mere catchwords for the eye.
28683 In manuscripts (Greek, Latin, Sanskrit) written with
28684 letters of one size, with no separation of words, and
28685 with very few stops, these particles serve the pur-
28686 pose effected now-a-days by capitals, by division or
28687 spacing, and by punctuation. In spoken language it
28688 is often wrong to render them otherwise than by
28689 inflection or by stress of voice.</p>
28690 <p>{@14.@} U. f. {@vaí.@} --See {@√brū + apa.@}</p>
28691 <p>{@14-15.@} U. f. {@tā́m yád ápṛchan@} (207), {@sā́@}
28692 {@abravīt: “adyá amṛta” íti. té abruvan:@}
28693 {@“ná vaí iyám@} etc.” --Difference between
28694 imperf. {@amriyata@} and aor. {@amṛta@} (834a)
28695 illustrated at 928^3^. --Accent of {@tè,@} Whit-
28696 ney 84d, 135.</p>
28697 <p>{@15-16.@} Lit. ‘Not (if things keep on) in
28698 this way ({@itthám@}) does she forget him.’</p>
28699 <p>{@16.@} Note the fine distinction. The gods
28700 use the solemn old Vedic form {@rā́trīm;@} the
28701 narrator, the later and more colloquial
[Page393-b+ 50]
28702 {@rā́trim.@} Similar distinction at ŚB. xi. 5. 4^4^:
28703 cf. 98^20^N., 103^14^N. Not uncommon is the
28704 assumption that the gods have words or a
28705 dialect peculiar to themselves. Thus {@ná@}
28706 with the gods means the same as {@iva,@} AB.
28707 ii. 2. 14, 15. Cf. Iliad i. 403, ii. 814, xiv. 291,
28708 xx. 74, Od. x. 305; and A. F. Pott's {%Anti-%}
28709 {%kaulen,%} p. 71.</p>

28789 <p>SELECTION LXVI. TS. ii. 6. 6. Legend of
28790 Agni the oblation-bearer, and of the fish.--
28791 This is the Brāhmaṇa form of the myth which
28792 is the subject of RV. x. 51, and is adverted
28793 to in x. 52 = selection lvi. To their version
28794 of x. 51, GKR. add on p. 106 a translation of

28796 rendered by Muir, v. 203, and by Eggeling,
28797 SBE. xii. 452. Ludwig, v. 504-5, gives other
28798 Brāhmaṇa forms of this myth: cf. esp. ŚB.
28799 i. 2. 3^1^ = SBE. xii. 47. For Epic forms of the
28800 same, see Ad. Holtzmann, {%Agni nach den%}
[Page394-b+ 49]
28801 {%Vorstellungen des MBh.,%} p. 11, and esp. MBh.
28802 iii. 222. 7 = 14214f.</p>
28803 <p>{@14-15. tráyas,@} 482c. {@--√2mi + pra,@} 770a.</p>
28804 <p>{@15-16.@} ‘Agni feared, (thinking,) “In this
28805 way, surely, he ({@syás@}) will get into trouble, i. e.
28806 if things keep on in this way, I shall get into
28807 trouble.”’ He speaks of himself here (as
28808 also at x. 51. 6^a^) in the 3d person ({@syás@}). Note
28809 the common root of {@ā́_artim@} and {@ā́_ariṣyati.@}</p>
28810 <p>{@16. nílāyata:@} in strictness, to be divided
28811 thus, {@níl-āyata,@} ‘he went out, took himself
28812 off, hid.’ This is for {@nír a-ayata,@} an imperf.
28813 mid. of {@√i@} (after the model of {@a-jayata@} from
28814 {@√ji@}) with the prefix nis or nir; for accord-
28815 ing to Pāṇini (viii. 2. 19), the {@r@} of a preposi-
28816 tion with forms of the verb-stem aya is
28817 regularly changed to 1--cf. 1087c.</p>
28818 <p>In the Hindu mind, these forms of the
28819 verb-stem {@nil-aya@} were evidently confused
28820 with those of {@ni-laya,@} which yield a like mg
28821 --see BR. under {@√lī + ni.@} On this account,
28822 doubtless, the {@pada-pāṭha,@} which usually
28823 gives the division of compound verbs, re-
28824 frains here. The confusion is further at-
28825 tested by the analogous passage of the ŚB.,
28826 which has, in the Mādhyaṃdina text (i. 2. 3^1^),
28827 {@ni-lilye,@} and in the Kāṇva text, {@ni-layāṃ@}
28828 {@cakre@}--see SBE. xii. p. xlvi. The proper
28829 form from {@√lī@} in the passage before us would
28830 of course be {@ny-àlayata.@}</p>
28831 <p>{@16-17.@} U. f. {@pra_éṣam@} (see {@√2iṣ + pra@} 2 and
28832 ref.) {@aicchan@} ({@√1iṣ,@} 585). In the metaphor,
28833 Agni is implicitly likened to a hunted beast.</p>
28834 <p>{@17-18. tám aśapat@} etc., ‘Him (the fish)
28835 he (Agni) cursed (as follows): {@“dhiyā́…@}
28836 {@pra_ávocas.”@}’ See {@dhī́@} 1. {@--vadhyāsus@}
28837 (form ! 924) and {@ghnanti@} (637) have as sub-
28838 ject ‘people.’ --The loose use of {@sás, tám,@}
28839 etc., is one of the chief stylistic faults of the
28840 Brāhmaṇas.</p>

28854 <p>SELECTION LXVII. AB. iii. 20. Legend
28855 of Indra and the Maruts, and Vṛtra.--Trans-
28856 lated, Muir, v. 93. In selection xlvii. = RV.
28857 viii. 85. 7f, the Maruts are praised because
28858 they stood by Indra when all the other gods
28859 forsook him. The passage before us is an
28860 expansion of that myth, a “reproduction
28861 plus on moins amplifiée d'un cliché emprunté
28862 au livre des hymnes.”</p>
28863 <p>From other passages, it would appear that
28864 the Maruts also were faithless, cf. Muir, v.
28865 92 and 82^1^N. Both views are involved in the
28866 explanation of the Mid-day Soma Feast, ŚB.
28867 iv. 3. 3^6^f, where the Maruts first withdraw
28868 from Indra and afterwards help him (SBE.
28869 xxvi. 334f).</p>

28907 <p>{@15.@} U. f. {@“yad eva etad avocas.”@} The
28908 {@etad,@} q. v., goes appositively with {@yad,@} mark-
28909 ing the thing designated by {@yad@} as something
28910 preceding, and so may be rendered by ‘just’
28911 or ‘a moment ago.’ P. asks, ‘“Who am I,
28912 then ?” “Exactly what thou just saidst,”’
28913 replied Indra.</p>

28915 got the name of Ka. (For) P. is Ka by
28916 name = has the name Ka.’ Note that the
28917 predicate comes first.</p>

28922 mological explanations of the Brāhmaṇas:
28923 cf. 64^7^N.</p>

29011 <p>{@5.@} U. f. {@te…yajñakratūn tenire@} (794e).</p>
29012 <p>{@5f.@} THE SACRIFICES are described by
29013 Weber, ISt. x. 321f. The Hindus did not
29014 class them according to their purpose, as
29015 thank-offerings, expiatory offerings, etc.
29016 They grouped them</p>
29017 <p>{@A.@} according to the MATERIAL used, as:
29018 1. oblations of milk, ghee, corn; 2. animal
29019 sacrifices; 3. libations of Soma. And again</p>
29020 <p>{@B.@} according to the TIME, as: 1. at the
29021 beginning (x. 328) of each day and of each
29022 night ({@agnihotra@}); 2. at the beg. (x. 329) of
29023 the lunar half-month; 3. at the beg. (x. 337)
29024 of the three seasons,--spring, rains, autumn;
29025 4. at the beg. (x. 343) of the two harvests.
29026 The offering of first-fruits or {@nava-sasya_@}
29027 {@iṣṭi;@} in the spring, of barley; in the autumn,
29028 of rice; 5. at the beg. (x. 344) of the solar
29029 half-year, the {@paśu-bandha;@} 6. at the beg.
29030 (x. 352) of the new year, the Soma-sacrifice.</p>
29031 <p>With this last, often occurs the elaborate
29032 ceremony of building the fire-altar of bricks,
29033 ISt. xiii. 217-292. This ceremony is called
29034 the ‘Fire-piling,’ {@agni-cayana@} (see {@√1ci@}), or
29035 briefly agni.</p>
29036 <p>Schröder gives in brief compass a sketch
29037 of a specimen-sacrifice, ILuC. p. 97-109.</p>

29041 (94^18^N.) is doubtless a late notion. The path

29133 <p>{@16.@} Starting from the 3d pers. s. pres.
29134 ind. act. of a verb-root (e. g. {@śamnāti@} from
29135 {@√3śam@}), and treating it as a declinable noun-
29136 stem, like {@mati,@} the Hindu forms an ablative
29137 sing., e. g. {@śamnātes,@} to express “derivation
29138 from a root.” Render: ‘{@śarīra@} is from the
29139 root {@śṛ@} {%break,%} or from the root {@śam@} {%harm.%}’
29140 So with {@drāghati@} and the following two.</p>
29141 <p>{@19f.@} After the verbal explanations, comes
29142 the mythological discussion. ‘Who then is
29143 Vṛtra ? “A cloud” say the etymologists.
[Page398-a+ 52]
29144 “An Asura descended from Tvaṣṭar” say
29145 the tellers of old legends.’ There were, then,
29146 already schools of conflicting opinions. Cf.
29147 Muir, ii^3^. 170f.</p>

29157 <p>SELECTION LXXIV. Wedding-customs
29158 and the wedding-service. Āśvalāyana Gṛhya-
29159 sūtra, book i., chap's 5, 7, 8.--Stenzler pub-
29160 lished the text in the {%Abhandlungen für die%}
29161 {%Kunde des Morgenlandes,%} vol. iii., 1864; and
29162 the translation, vol. iv., 1865. Cf. Weber,
29163 {%Indische Streifen,%} ii. 296f. The text appeared
29164 with a Hindu comment in the {%Bibliotheca%}
29165 {%Indica,%} 1866-69. English translation by
29166 Oldenberg, SBE. xxix. 159f.</p>
29167 <p>--On the subject-matter of this selection,
29168 the following essays and books may be con-
29169 sulted. The most important is the essay of
29170 Haas, with additions by Weber, ISt. v. 267-
29171 410; cf. esp. the synoptic index, 410-12.
29172 Haas gives the text and an annotated trans-
29173 lation of our selection at pages 289f, 362f.</p>
29174 <p>See also notes to selection lviii., p. 389.
29175 Further, Kaegi, 74 (102), and notes; Zimmer,
29176 309f; Kaegi in {%Fleckeisen's Jahrbücher,%} 1880,
29177 456f; and Colebrooke's {%Essays,%} i. 217-38.</p>
29178 <p>--Birth, reproduction, and death are the three
29179 great facts of all organic life. It is therefore natural
29180 that the customs connected with marriage and burial
29181 should take so important a place in the traditions
29182 of primitive peoples. It can hardly be doubted that
29183 a considerable body of these customs have their root
29184 in Indo-European antiquity. For we find, as between
29185 the various members of this family, many and most
29186 striking coincidences of usage. The systematic ex-
29187 position and criticism of these coincidences form one
29188 of the most interesting chapters of comparative phil-
29189 ology. It is not feasible to point them out in detail
29190 here. In lien of this may be cited--</p>
29191 <p>For purposes of comparative study: Joa-
29192 chim Marquardt, {%Privatleben der Römer,%} i^2^.
29193 28f; A. Rossbach, {%Die Römische Ehe,%} Stutt-
29194 gart, 1853; G. F. Schömann, {%Griechische%}
29195 {%Alterthümer^2^,%} ii. 529-36; K. Weinhold, {%Die%}
[Page398-b+ 52]
29196 {%Deutschen Frauen%} (Wien, 1851), p. 190-274, or
29197 {%Altnordisches Leben%} (Berlin, 1856), 238-59;
29198 Spiegel, {%Erānische Alterthumskunde,%} iii. 676-81.</p>
29199 <p>--SYNOPSIS of the subject-matter (with
29200 references to passages in vol. v. of the ISt.,
29201 where Haas and Weber treat of the Hindu
29202 customs or cite analogous ones):--</p>
29203 <p>CHAPTER v. Test of the bride by means
29204 of exorcised lumps of earth. See ISt. v. 288f.</p>
29205 <p>CHAP. vii. The marriage ceremony.
29206 Dextrarum iunctio (v. 277, 311). Bride led
29207 around the fire and water (v. 318N. 2, 396N.).
29208 Amo {@'ham asmi@} (v. 216). Mounting the stone
29209 (v. 318N. 1). Oblation (v. 318N. 3). Loosing
29210 braids (v. 320). Seven steps (v. 320f, 321N.).</p>
29211 <p>CHAP. viii. Wedding journey (v. 327f).
29212 Arrival at new home (v. 329). Pellis lanata
29213 (Rossbach, 113f, 324; Marquardt, 50). Con-
29214 tinence (v. 325f, 331).</p>

29216 of a stanza is quoted, the entire stanza is
29217 meant. If only part of the first pāda of
29218 a hymn is quoted, the entire hymn is meant.

29221 AGS. i. 20. 9.</p>
