29302 <head>NOTES TO PAGE {@99.@}</head>
29303 <p>{@1. pari-ṇayam:@} we should read {@pari-ṇay-@}
29304 {@añ@} or (BI.) {@-an,@} pres. ppl. --‘Leading (her)
29305 thrice to the right around the fire and the
29306 water-jar.’ The analogies are remarkable:
29307 cf. <gr></gr>, the Roman {%dextratio,%} the Gaelic
29308 “walking the deasil,” etc. Consult SBE.
29309 xii. 37, 45, 272, 442; Rossbach, 231, 314f:
29310 Marquardt, i^2^. 51 and N. 1. Circumambula-
29311 tions followed the course of the sun on
29312 occasions of joy; and were reversed (104^21^)
29313 on occasions of sorrow.</p>
29314 <p>{@2-3.@} Pronounce: {@sā tvam asi; amo@}
29315 {@aham.@} --These interesting formulae occur
29316 at AV. xiv. 2. 71; ŚB. xiv. 9. 4^19^; PGS. i. 6. 3;
29317 AB. viii. 27; ŚGS. i. 13. 4. For {@pāda@} {%d,%} the
29318 first three have {@sāmāham asmi; ṛk tuam.@}</p>
29319 <p>--The {@sāman@} is conceived as male (ŚB.
29320 iv. 6. 7^11^), and as sprung from the {@ṛc@} (as it is),
29321 or as husband of it (ŚB. viii. 1. 3^5^). But to
29322 the Hindu mind this lugging in of {@sāma@} has
29323 a charming mystic significance, inasmuch as
29324 {@sā@} plus {@ama@} makes {@sāma@} (see AB. iii. 23;
29325 and SBE. i. 13). --The conception of heaven
29326 as male and of earth as female is common
29327 --see Preller, {%Gr. Mythologie^3^,%} i. 37f.</p>
29328 <p>--The Vedic formula has a general sig-
29329 nificance not unlike that of the ancient
29330 {%quando%} (or {%ubi%}) {%tu Gaius ego Gaia%} and the
29331 German {%Wo ich Mann bin, da bist du Frau,%}
29332 {%und wo du Frau bist, da bin ich Mann.%} For
29333 the Latin formula, see Rossbach, p. 351;
29334 ISt. v. 216; Fleckeisen, 1880, p. 457; and
29335 esp. the discussion by Marquardt, i^2^. 49N. 2.
29336 For the German, see ISt. v. 216. Another
29337 use of the Vedic formula, ISt. x. 160.</p>
29338 <p>{@4-5. ehi,@} used just like <gr></gr> or <gr></gr>; but
29339 cf. the variants noted ISt. v. 332N. --If we
29340 could read {@priyau,@} the metre would be in
29341 order (8 + 8 + 11 + 8); but cf. TS. iv. 2. 5^1^.</p>
29342 <p>{@6.@} Force of repetition--‘With each lead-
29343 ing-around’ (1260). --She mounts the stone
29344 or puts her foot on it as a symbol of the
29345 way in which she is to put her foot on her
29346 enemies.</p>
[Page400-a+ 50]
29347 <p>{@9.@} U. f. {@vadhv-añjalau@} (134 end) {@upa-@}
29348 {@stīrya@} (see {@√stṛ + upa@}). The loc. is adjunct
29349 of {@ā-vapati@} as well as of the gerund.</p>
29350 <p>{@9-11.@} The first pouring ({@upastaraṇa@}) of
29351 {@ājya@} and the two strewings of parched grain
29352 on the bride's hands, and the second sprin-
29353 kling ({@pratyabhighāraṇa@}) of {@ājya,@} constitute
29354 the four portions “cut off” or separated
29355 from the {@havis@} or sacrificial food. The first
29356 is done by the groom; the rest by the brother.
29357 The descendants of Jamadagni used to “cut
29358 off” five such portions (ISt. v. 366; x. 95)
29359 and so had to strew grain three times.--
29360 Oldenberg's note. {@--eṣas,@} refers back.</p>
29361 <p>--‘(The groom) having poured the sacri-
29362 ficial butter on the bride's hollowed-and-
29363 joined-hands, her brother or brother's repre-
29364 sentative strews parched grain (on her hands)
29365 twice [Thrice (is the custom) of the Jamad-
29366 agnians.], sprinkling again (sc. {@ājya@}) over
29367 the {@havis@} (= what he has left of the grain in
29368 the basket) and over the {@avatta@} (= what grain
29369 he has strewn on her hands). This (as just
29370 stated) is the cutting-off-usage.’</p>
29371 <p>{@12-17. ayakṣata,@} 882. {@--pra…muñcātu@}
29372 (cf. 89^9^), impv., w. lengthening (ISt. v. 340N.):
29373 so {@nudātu, svadātu.@} --These stanzas are
29374 mere adaptations of blank forms, so to say.
29375 For examples of the changes (called {@ūhās@})
29376 which circumstances demand, see AB. ii. 6. 6;
29377 cf. AGS. iii. 8. 7. The forms are filled out
29378 (see {@nigama@} in BR.) with a deity-name,
29379 which, as here, does not always fit the metre.</p>
29380 <p>{@18.@} ‘With the above mantras (uttered
29381 by the groom), she, not-parting (fem.) her
29382 joined-hands, should offer (the grain in
29383 them), as with a {@sruc.@}’ The nose of the
29384 {@sruc@} is at the side. She is therefore to pour
29385 out the grain so,--not over her finger-tips.</p>
29386 <p>--For the case of families who do not
29387 strew grain but twice, we must assume that
29388 the bride, when making her second oblation,
29389 does not offer all the grain in her joined-
29390 hands, but leaves some for her third oblation.
29391 Her fourth is from the basket.</p>
29392 <p>{@18-19@} (§ 14). ‘Without (any) leading
29393 around (on the part of the groom), (the
29394 bride should offer grain) with the nose of
29395 the basket towards herself in silence the
29396 fourth time.’ The “silence” refers of course
[Page400-b+ 53]
29397 to the groom. The bride does not say any-
29398 thing at any time (cf. SBE. xxix. 37).</p>
29399 <p>--As prescribed above, the rites are per-
29400 formed in the order following:</p>
29401 <head>I. II. First and second rounds:</head>
29402 <p>{%a.%} leading around, § 6;</p>
29403 <p>{%b.%} mounting stone, § 7;</p>
29404 <p>{%c.%} strewing grain, § 8;</p>
29405 <p>{%d.%} oblation w. mantra 1 or 2, § 13.</p>
29406 <head>III. Third round:</head>
29407 <p>{%a.%} leading around;</p>
29408 <p>{%b.%} mounting stone;</p>
29409 <p>{%c.%} sprinkling ājya, § 10;</p>
29410 <p>{%d.%} oblation w. mantra 3, § 13.</p>
29411 <head>IV. Fourth time:</head>
29412 <p>{%a, b,%} and {%c%} fall out;</p>
29413 <p>{%d.%} oblation in silence.</p>
29414 <p>{@19-20.@} U. f. {@ā_upya ā_upya@} (127 end)
29415 {@ha@} (see {@ha@}) {@eke@} etc. ‘Some lead her around
29416 after each strewing (of) the grain. In this
29417 way ({@tathā@}) the last two oblations do not
29418 fall together.’ That is, some do the rites in
29419 the order:
29420 {%c.%} strewing or sprinkling;
29421 {%d.%} oblation w. mantra;
29422 {%a.%} leading around;
29423 {%b.%} mounting stone.
29424 In this way, the fourth oblation comes directly
29425 after the third mounting of the stone.</p>
29426 <p>{@20-21. asyai,@} 365. 3. ‘Then he loosens
29427 her two braids, if they are made; (i. e. if)
29428 two braids of wool at her two temples are
29429 tied.’</p>
29430 <p>{@22.@} He loosens the right one with RV.
29431 x. 85. 24. {@--uttarām@} (sc. {@śikhām@}) {@uttarayā@}
29432 (sc. {@ṛcā@}): see {@uttara@} 3, 4.</p>

29438 <p>{@1-3.@} U. f. {@iṣe ekapadī@} (sc. {@bhava@}), etc.,
29439 ‘be taking one step for strength = take one
29440 step for strength; two for vigor;’ and so on.
29441 --The body consists of {%five%} elements. At
29442 one period, the seasons are reckoned as
29443 {%six:%} {@śíśira, vasantá, grīṣmá, varṣā́, śarád,@}
29444 {@hemantá.@}</p>
29445 <p>{@4.@} See {@saptapada@} in vocab. Seven, as a
29446 sacred number, became the symbol for
29447 ‘many’ or ‘all.’ Doubtless the word always
29448 suggested both the literal and the trans-
29449 ferred mgs. Orig., {@sakhā saptapado bhava,@}
[Page401-a+ 50]
29450 ‘Be a constant friend,’ was a mere general
29451 formula, used on sealing a friendship, e. g.
29452 between two men, who would take seven
29453 steps together hand in hand by way of rati-
29454 fying their bond. {@Sāptapadam maitram,@}
29455 ‘Friendship (if genuine) is constant,’ be-
29456 came a common proverb.</p>
29457 <p>--The application of the formula to the
29458 wedding-ceremony is prob. only a secondary
29459 and special use; although it came to be
29460 exceedingly important. Here {@saptapadī@}
29461 being taken with strict literalness, it became
29462 necessary to lead up to it by six other
29463 formulae--as in the text. The matter is
29464 explained at length and illustrated, by Haas
29465 and Weber, ISt. v. 320-22: cf. BR. s. v.
29466 {@saptapada;@} and AV. v. 11. 10.</p>

29522 reward of such self-restraint.’</p>
29523 <head>NOTES TO PAGE {@101.@}</head>
29524 <p>{@1.@} Marital intercourse is declared by Āpa-
29525 stamba to be a duty resting on the authority
29526 of Holy Writ ({@brāhmaṇa-vacanāc ca saṃ-@}
29527 {@veśanam,@} ii. 1. 19 = SBE. ii. 101). The Scrip-
29528 ture-passage, acc. to Bühler, is TS. ii. 5. 1^5^,
29529 {@kā́mam ā́ víjanitoḥ sám bhavāma,@} ‘Let us
29530 have intercourse after our heart's desire till
29531 a child be begotten.’ Explicit is Baudhāyana,
29532 iv. 1. 17 = SBE. xiv. 315; MBh. xii. 21. 12 = 626.
29533 Cf. Ludwig, v. 549 (N. to RV. i. 179. 2), and iv.
29534 315: also Exodus xxi. 10f, and I Cor. vii. 3.</p>
29535 <p>{@2.@} See {@√vac,@} {%caus:%} form, cf. 98^20^N.</p>
29536 <p>SELECTION LXXV. The customs and
29537 ritual of cremation and burial. Āśvalāyana
29538 Gṛhya-sūtra, b'k iv., chap's 1-6.--Text and
29539 translations as at the beginning of intro-
29540 duction to selection lxxiv. Roth compares
29541 the ceremonies here described with those
29542 implied by the text of RV. x. 18 in his essay,
29543 {%die Todtenbestattung im indischen Alterthum,%}
29544 ZDMG. viii. 467-75, reprinted in part by Zim-
29545 mer, p. 404f. The same subject is treated
29546 at length by Max Müller, ZDMG. ix. p. I-
29547 LXXXII. We may mention also Colebrooke's
[Page402-a+ 55]
29548 {%Essays,%} i. 172-95; the papers of Wilson and
29549 of Rājendralāla Mitra, cited above, p. 382f;
29550 and especially Monier-Williams, in {%Religious%}
29551 {%Thought and Life in India,%} chap. xi., {%Death,%}
29552 {%Funeral Rites, and Ancestor-worship,%} and in
29553 {%Ind. Ant.%} v. 27. Cf. also in general the intro-
29554 duction to selection liii., p. 382f.</p>
29555 <p>The ceremonies in question have three
29556 main parts: the cremation; the gathering
29557 and burial of the bones; and the expiation.
29558 --These are followed by the {@śrāddha,@} de-
29559 scribed at AGS. iv. 7, SBE. xxix. 250f, 106f.</p>

29761 <p>{@1.@} More fully, {@krītena vā, utpannena vā@}
29762 (sc. {@annena@}): see {@√pad + ud.@}</p>
29763 <p>{@3f.@} Render the locatives by ‘in case of,’
29764 i. e. here ‘in case of the death of.’ {@--dāna_@}
29765 {@adhyayane,@} acc. dual n., 1253a.</p>
29766 <p>{@8.@} See {@ūrdhvam.@} --‘Tenth’ (see {@daśamī@})
29767 --counting from the day of death. {@--@}
29768 {@kṛṣṇapakṣasya ayujāsu,@} sc. {@tithiṣu.@} --See
29769 {@eka-nakṣatra.@} Of the 28 lunar mansions,
29770 six form three pairs, named ‘former’ and
29771 ‘latter’ Phalgunī (9-10), Aṣāḍhā (18-19),
29772 and Bhadrapadā (24-25). Accordingly,
29773 under these asterisms, or in the lunar months
29774 named after them, the gathering is forbidden.</p>
29775 <p>See Whitney, OLSt. ii. 351f, 360. But cf.
29776 Weber, {%Abh. der Berliner Akad.,%} 1861, p. 322.</p>
29777 <p>{@9.@} ‘In a plain male urn (they put) a man
29778 (i. e. his bones); in a plain female (urn--sc.
29779 {@kumbhyām@}), a woman.’ If the urn has pro-
29780 tuberances on it, like a woman's breasts, it
29781 is regarded as a female urn. Many such
29782 have been found by Schliemann--see his
29783 {%Ilios,%} numbers 986, 988-93. A male urn is
29784 one without these breasts.</p>
29785 <p>{@9-10.@} § 3, cf. 101^17^. {@--prasavyam,@} 99^1^N.</p>
29786 <p>{@11-12.@} U. f. {@aṅguṣṭha_upakaniṣṭhikā-@}
29787 {@bhyām@} (cf. 105^16^) {@ekaikam asthi a-@} etc.
29788 Even the Brāhmaṇas give evidence of a well-
29789 developed body of popular beliefs about
29790 the fingers: cf. ŚB. iii. 1. 3^25^; iii. 3. 2^2, 13^f, and
29791 Eggeling's Index, SBE. xxvi. 461, s. v. {%fingers.%}
29792 See the beautiful essay of W. Grimm, {%Ueber%}
29793 {%die bedeutung der deutschen fingernamen, Klein-%}
29794 {%ere Schriften,%} iii. 425-50.</p>
29795 <p>But with the finger next the little one is
29796 associated--now something mysterious, now
29797 something uncanny (as here): this appears
29798 from the fact that it is the ‘nameless’ one
[Page404-b+ 48]
29799 not only in Sanskrit ({@a-nāmikā@}), but also
29800 with Tibetans, Chinese, Mongols, Lithua-
29801 nians, Finus, and North American Indians.
29802 See Grimm, l. c. 441-47; and 91^2^N.</p>

29805 small bones yet remaining among the ashes,

29807 a ‘fan to blow the ashes from the carefully

29876 <p>{@3.@} The scholiast to AGS. ii. 3 end, enu-
29877 merates the “Sun-hymns” and the “Bless-
29878 ings.” The “Sun-hymns” are RV. x. 158;

29882 is given in the Reader--see Preface, page v,
